class=\"toolbar-wrap js-toolbar-wrap\">\r\n                <div class=\"toolbar-inner-wrap\">\r\n                    <ul id=\"Toolbar\" role=\"navigation\">\r\n    <li class=\"toolbar-item </div>\r\n                </div>\r\n            </div>\r\n            <div id=\"ContentTab\" class=\"content active\">\r\n    <div class=\"widget widget-ArticleFulltext widget-instance-OUP_Article_FullText_Widget\">\n        <div class=\"module-widget\">\r\n    <div class=\"widget-items\" data-widgetname=\"ArticleFulltext\">\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n<section class=\"abstract\"><p class=\"chapter-para\">We have determined the mass density radial profiles of the first five strong gravitational lens systems discovered by the <em>Herschel</em> Astrophysical Terahertz Large Area Survey. We present an enhancement of the semilinear lens inversion method of Warren &amp; Dye which allows simultaneous reconstruction of several different wavebands and apply this to dual-band imaging of the lenses acquired with the <em>Hubble Space Telescope</em>. The five systems analysed here have lens redshifts which span a range 0.22 \u2264 | $z$ | \u2264 0.94. Our findings are consistent with other studies by concluding that: (1) the logarithmic slope of the total mass density profile steepens with decreasing redshift; (2) the slope is positively correlated with the average total projected mass density of the lens contained within half the effective radius and negatively correlated with the effective radius; (3) the fraction of dark matter contained within half the effective radius increases with increasing effective radius and increases with redshift.</p></section>                        <div class=\"article-metadata-panel clearfix\"></div>\r\n<div class=\"kwd-group\"><a class=\"kwd-part kwd-main\" href=\"javascript:;\" data-keyword=\"&quot;galaxies: evolution&quot;\">galaxies: evolution</a>, <a class=\"kwd-part kwd-main\" href=\"javascript:;\" data-keyword=\"&quot;galaxies: structure&quot;\">galaxies: structure</a></div>                    <h2 scrollto-destination=111789702 id=\"111789702\" class=\"section-title\" data-legacy-id=sec1>1 INTRODUCTION</h2>\r\n<p class=\"chapter-para\">Early-type galaxies, despite being relatively well studied, continue to challenge our complete understanding of their formation and evolution. Current unanswered questions include quantifying the role of mergers in their evolution (e.g. van Dokkum et\u00a0al. <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib74\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib74\" data-open=\"bib74\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">1999</a>; Khochfar &amp; Burkert <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib38\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib38\" data-open=\"bib38\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2003</a>; Bell et\u00a0al. <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib5\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib5\" data-open=\"bib5\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2006</a>; Hilz, Naab &amp; Ostriker <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib34\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib34\" data-open=\"bib34\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2013</a>), reliably determining their stellar build-up and reconciling this with downsizing (e.g. Thomas et\u00a0al. <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib66\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib66\" data-open=\"bib66\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2005</a>; Maraston et\u00a0al. <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib46\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib46\" data-open=\"bib46\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2009</a>; Tojeiro et\u00a0al. <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib67\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib67\" data-open=\"bib67\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2012</a>), understanding the evolution of the upper end of the mass function (e.g. Bundy, Ellis &amp; Conselice <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib12\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib12\" data-open=\"bib12\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2005</a>; Bundy, Treu &amp; Ellis <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib13\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib13\" data-open=\"bib13\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2007</a>; van der Wel et\u00a0al. <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib73\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib73\" data-open=\"bib73\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2009</a>; Hopkins et\u00a0al. <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib35\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib35\" data-open=\"bib35\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2010</a>) and identifying the process(es) by which the high-redshift population becomes dramatically less compact at low redshifts (e.g. Daddi et\u00a0al. <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib21\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib21\" data-open=\"bib21\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2005</a>; Trujillo et\u00a0al. <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib72\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib72\" data-open=\"bib72\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2006</a>; van Dokkum et\u00a0al. <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib75\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib75\" data-open=\"bib75\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2008</a>; Lani et\u00a0al. <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib41\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib41\" data-open=\"bib41\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2013</a>). Regarding this last point, mergers have been suggested as the cause of the effect, but there is much disagreement (e.g. Hopkins et\u00a0al. <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib35\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib35\" data-open=\"bib35\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2010</a>; Oser et\u00a0al. <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib52\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib52\" data-open=\"bib52\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2012</a>). Notwithstanding these unknowns, it seems likely that common formation and evolution mechanisms are at play, given the tightness of observed relationships such as the Fundamental Plane, the correlation of black hole mass with central velocity dispersion and the near-isothermality of total mass density profiles.</p><p class=\"chapter-para\">Regarding the measurement of density profiles, this has recently become a very active pursuit within the field, motivated by the many scientific applications made possible. These applications include the provision of an observational benchmark for simulations of large-scale structure formation, constraining the initial mass function (IMF) by comparing with stellar synthesis masses (e.g. Auger et\u00a0al. <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib2\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib2\" data-open=\"bib2\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2010a</a>; Treu et\u00a0al. <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib70\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib70\" data-open=\"bib70\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2010</a>; Barnab\u00e8 et\u00a0al. <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib4\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib4\" data-open=\"bib4\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2013</a>), determining the Hubble constant from gravitational lens time delays (e.g. Tewes et\u00a0al. <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib65\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib65\" data-open=\"bib65\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2013</a>, see also the review by Jackson <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib36\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib36\" data-open=\"bib36\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2007</a>) and cosmography (see Treu <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib68\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib68\" data-open=\"bib68\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2010b</a>, and references therein). Other novel applications include using density profiles of strongly lensed systems embedded within a cluster or group environment as a direct probe of the larger scale gravitational potential (e.g. Dye et\u00a0al. <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib27\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib27\" data-open=\"bib27\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2007</a>; Limousin et\u00a0al. <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib42\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib42\" data-open=\"bib42\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2010</a>) and making predictions of the self-annihilation signal of dark matter to guide annihilation detection experiments (e.g. Walker et\u00a0al. <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib78\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib78\" data-open=\"bib78\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2011</a>).</p><p class=\"chapter-para\">A debate that continues to be rekindled is the issue of whether density profiles are cored, whereby the density tends to a constant value towards small radii or whether they are cuspy, whereby the density continues to increase as a radial power law. To the advocates of cuspy profiles, the debate is over the extent to which they are cuspy, i.e. the exponent of the radial power law. The motivation that drives these studies originates from comparing observed density profiles with those predicted by <em>N</em>-body simulations of large-scale structure formation.</p><p class=\"chapter-para\">Early simulations favoured cuspy profiles that are typically steeper than those inferred from observations (see, for example, de Blok &amp; Bosma <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib22\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib22\" data-open=\"bib22\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2002</a>, and citations to this work). However, these early studies were largely based on pure dark matter simulations which ignored the effects of baryons. Accordingly, the observational data concentrated on dwarf galaxies where baryons behave more like test particles in a dominating dark matter potential. Recent work by Cole et\u00a0al. (<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib19\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib19\" data-open=\"bib19\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2012</a>) shows that cuspy dark matter profiles in simulated dwarf spheroidals results in stronger dynamical friction causing globular clusters to fall into the centres on a dynamical time-scale, in contrast to what is observed in these systems.</p><p class=\"chapter-para\">Simulations of large-scale structure are now beginning to incorporate baryons, but this is a highly complex task, fraught with many complicating factors such as black hole accretion and their subsequent feedback (e.g. Croton <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib20\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib20\" data-open=\"bib20\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2006</a>; Ciotti, Ostriker &amp; Proga <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib18\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib18\" data-open=\"bib18\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2009</a>; Bryan et\u00a0al. <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib11\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib11\" data-open=\"bib11\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2013</a>), feedback from supernovae and cooling (see, for example, Duffy et\u00a0al. <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib24\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib24\" data-open=\"bib24\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2010</a>; Newton &amp; Kay <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib49\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib49\" data-open=\"bib49\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2013</a>). Understanding the interplay of baryons and dark matter is not only essential to a full comprehension of the formation and evolution of galaxies, but can also shed light on the properties of the dark matter itself, such as constraining the self-interaction cross-section of dark matter (Spergel &amp; Steinhardt <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib63\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib63\" data-open=\"bib63\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2000</a>; Loeb &amp; Weiner <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib43\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib43\" data-open=\"bib43\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2011</a>). In this regard, Lovell et\u00a0al. (<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib44\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib44\" data-open=\"bib44\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2012</a>) find that the velocity profiles of satellite galaxies around the Milky Way are considerably better matched by warm dark matter density profiles than cold dark matter density profiles.</p><p class=\"chapter-para\">Improving the quality of observations of density profiles of galaxies, particularly the more poorly understood early types, therefore provides a much needed benchmark to assist in discrimination of the many different scenarios describing their history. Gravitational lensing offers a very powerful and yet conceptually simple approach to achieving this, independent of assumptions about the kinematical state of the deflecting mass. Whilst weak galaxy\u2013galaxy lensing can be used to constrain density profiles, this, by necessity, must be conducted in a statistical sense (e.g. Velander et\u00a0al. <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib76\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib76\" data-open=\"bib76\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2014</a>) and provides measurements of the density profile on larger scales where the dark matter dominates. Conversely, strong galaxy\u2013galaxy lensing is applicable on a per-galaxy basis and is sensitive to density profiles on small scales where the poorly constrained baryon physics is more dominant.</p><p class=\"chapter-para\">Early-type galaxies have a higher average lensing cross-section than disc galaxies (e.g. Maoz &amp; Rix <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib45\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib45\" data-open=\"bib45\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">1993</a>) and hence strong galaxy\u2013galaxy lens samples tend to harbour significantly more early- than late-type lenses. In this way, such samples provide a perfect opportunity to gain unique insights into the formation of early types, a fact that has inspired the culmination of several different lens samples to date. Recent strong galaxy\u2013galaxy lens samples include the Sloan Lens ACS (SLACS) survey (Bolton et\u00a0al. <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib7\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib7\" data-open=\"bib7\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2006</a>) with 85 lenses out to a redshift of | $z$ | \u2243 0.4 (median redshift \u22430.2; Auger et\u00a0al. <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib1\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib1\" data-open=\"bib1\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2009</a>), the Strong Lensing Legacy Survey (SL2S; Cabanac et\u00a0al. <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib15\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib15\" data-open=\"bib15\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2007</a>) with a final total of 36 lenses in the range 0.2 \u2264 | $z$ | \u2264 0.8 (median redshift \u22430.5; Sonnenfeld et\u00a0al. <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib62\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib62\" data-open=\"bib62\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2013</a>), the Sloan WFC Edge-on Late-type Lens Survey (SWELLS; Treu et\u00a0al. <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib71\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib71\" data-open=\"bib71\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2011</a>) with 20 disc galaxy lenses at | $z$ | \u2272 0.2 (Dutton et\u00a0al. <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib25\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib25\" data-open=\"bib25\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2013</a>) and 20 lenses at 0.4 \u2264 | $z$ | \u2264 0.7 identified in the Baryon Oscillation Spectroscopic Survey (BOSS; Eisenstein et\u00a0al. <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib30\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib30\" data-open=\"bib30\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2011</a>) which constitute the BOSS Emission-Line Lens Survey (BELLS; Brownstein et\u00a0al. <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib10\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib10\" data-open=\"bib10\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2012</a>). Regardless of their selection as lensing galaxies, both the SWELLS and the SLACS samples are found to be statistically consistent with being drawn at random from their parent unlensed samples with the same mass and redshift distributions (Bolton et\u00a0al. <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib8\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib8\" data-open=\"bib8\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2008</a>; Treu et\u00a0al. <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib71\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib71\" data-open=\"bib71\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2011</a>). This fortifies the role of lens samples in their aforementioned applications.</p><p class=\"chapter-para\">Analysis of these existing lens surveys has already enabled some interesting insights into galaxy evolution. For example, the fraction of dark matter within half the effective radius of early types increases with galaxy size and mass (Auger et\u00a0al. <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib3\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib3\" data-open=\"bib3\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2010b</a>; Ruff et\u00a0al. <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib57\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib57\" data-open=\"bib57\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2011</a>). Another intriguing result which has direct consequences for simulations of large-scale structure is that the total density profile of early-type galaxies appears to steepen with decreasing redshift (Ruff et\u00a0al. <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib57\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib57\" data-open=\"bib57\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2011</a>; Bolton et\u00a0al. <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib9\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib9\" data-open=\"bib9\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2012</a>; Sonnenfeld et\u00a0al. <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib62\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib62\" data-open=\"bib62\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2013</a>) although the degree to which this occurs is currently in disagreement.</p><p class=\"chapter-para\">In this paper, we present modelling of the first five strong galaxy\u2013galaxy lens systems identified in the <em>Herschel</em> Astrophysical Terahertz Large Area Survey (H-ATLAS; Eales et\u00a0al. <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib29\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib29\" data-open=\"bib29\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2010</a>). The H-ATLAS is a large-area survey (\u223c550 deg<sup>2</sup>) conducted in five passbands in the submillimetre (submm) wavelength range | $100\\lesssim \\lambda \\lesssim 500\\, \\mu$ |m using the <em>Herschel Space Observatory</em> (Pilbratt et\u00a0al. <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib54\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib54\" data-open=\"bib54\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2010</a>). Being a submm survey, the negative <em>K</em>-correction afforded by submm galaxies means that lensed sources are much more readily detected out to significantly greater redshifts than surveys conducted at optical wavelengths. Increasing the distance of a source increases the probability of it being lensed by intervening matter. Combining this fact with the large areal coverage of H-ATLAS results in an anticipated sample of hundreds of strong galaxy\u2013galaxy lenses (Negrello et\u00a0al. <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib48\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib48\" data-open=\"bib48\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2010</a>; Gonz\u00e1lez-Nuevo et\u00a0al. <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib32\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib32\" data-open=\"bib32\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2012</a>).</p><p class=\"chapter-para\">Such lenses also have the advantage that their submm emission is unaffected by any dust in the lens which means that a clean view of the source is obtained. This proves particularly important when reconstructing high-resolution surface brightness maps of the high-redshift lensed source for morphological studies. Furthermore, submm galaxy number counts are steep so that strongly lensed galaxies can be straightforwardly identified with simple flux selection criteria. This simple technique has also been applied by the Herschel Multi-tiered Extragalactic Survey (HerMES; Oliver et\u00a0al. <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib51\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib51\" data-open=\"bib51\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2012</a>. See Wardlow et\u00a0al. <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib79\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib79\" data-open=\"bib79\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2013</a>, for an account of the lensing aspects of this survey) and the survey carried out at mm wavelengths by the South Pole Telescope (Carlstrom et\u00a0al. <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib16\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib16\" data-open=\"bib16\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2011</a>; Vieira et\u00a0al. <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib77\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib77\" data-open=\"bib77\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2013</a>).</p><p class=\"chapter-para\">The ultimate size of the H-ATLAS sample is an obvious advantage in terms of improving statistical uncertainties. However, another far more compelling benefit that arises from the negative <em>K</em>-correction in the submm is that higher redshift lensed sources are more likely to be lensed by higher redshift lenses. From an evolutionary point of view, this brings about an increase in the period over which transformations in density profiles can be determined, back to earlier times in the Universe's history when the rate of galaxy evolution was stronger (for a theoretical perspective, see, for e.g. Schaye et\u00a0al. <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib59\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib59\" data-open=\"bib59\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2010</a>). In addition to this, almost all submm galaxies have extended structure on the scales of typical galaxy lens caustics so that their lensed images comprise extended arcs and ring-like structures. As demonstrated by Dye &amp; Warren (<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib26\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib26\" data-open=\"bib26\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2005</a>) in application of the semilinear inversion (SLI) algorithm (Warren &amp; Dye <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib80\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib80\" data-open=\"bib80\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2003</a>), extended structure in lensed images allows stronger constraints to be placed on the density profile of the lensing galaxy.</p><p class=\"chapter-para\">In this paper, we apply an enhanced version of the SLI method which allows multiple data sets observed at different wavelengths to be simultaneously reconstructed with the same lens model. We apply this to <em>Hubble Space Telescope</em> (<em>HST</em>) images acquired in both the <em>F</em>110\u8201<em>W</em> and <em>F</em>160\u8201<em>W</em> filters of each of the five lenses identified in the 14.4\u00a0deg<sup>2</sup> data released by the H-ATLAS consortium as science demonstration phase (SDP) data.</p><p class=\"chapter-para\">The layout of this paper is as follows: Section\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-sec2\"></span><a href=\"#sec2\" class=\"sectionLink xref-sec\">2</a> outlines the data. In Section\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-sec3\"></span><a href=\"#sec3\" class=\"sectionLink xref-sec\">3</a>, we describe the methodology of the lens modelling, including a description of the enhanced SLI method. Section\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-sec4\"></span><a href=\"#sec4\" class=\"sectionLink xref-sec\">4</a> presents the results and we summarize the findings of this work in Section\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-sec6\"></span><a href=\"#sec6\" class=\"sectionLink xref-sec\">6</a>. Throughout this paper, we assume the following cosmological parameters; <em>H</em><sub>0</sub>\u00a0=\u00a067\u8201km\u8201s<sup>\u22121</sup>\u8201Mpc<sup>\u22121</sup>, | $\\Omega _{\\rm m}=0.32$ |, \u03a9<sub>\u039b</sub>\u00a0=\u00a00.68 [Ade et\u00a0al. (Planck Collaboration) <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib1a\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib1a\" data-open=\"bib1a\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2013</a>].</p>                    <h2 scrollto-destination=111789716 id=\"111789716\" class=\"section-title\" data-legacy-id=sec2>2 DATA</h2>\r\n<p class=\"chapter-para\">The data analysed in this work are more thoroughly described in a companion paper (Negrello et\u00a0al. <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib49a\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib49a\" data-open=\"bib49a\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2014</a>, hereafter denoted <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib49a\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib49a\" data-open=\"bib49a\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">N14</a>) but we include the pertinent details here for completeness.</p><p class=\"chapter-para\">The <em>HST</em> observations were carried out in 2011 April in Cycle 18 under proposal 12194 (PI Negrello) using the Wide Field Camera 3 (WFC3). Two orbits were allocated per target with at least three quarters of the total exposure time per target of 5130\u00a0s acquired in the <em>F</em>160\u8201<em>W</em> filter and the remainder in the <em>F</em>110\u8201<em>W</em> filter. Images were reduced using the <span class=\"small-caps\">iraf</span><span class=\"small-caps\">multidrizzle</span> package and resampled to a pixel scale of 0.064\u00a0arcsec, half the intrinsic pixel scale of the WFC3.</p><p class=\"chapter-para\">The lens galaxy flux and lensed background source image in each system were then simultaneously fitted with smooth profiles using the <span class=\"small-caps\">galfit</span> software (Peng et\u00a0al. <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib53\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib53\" data-open=\"bib53\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2002</a>) and the lens profiles subtracted to leave the lensed image. These lens-subtracted images, along with their corresponding noise maps and point spread functions (PSFs) modelled by <span class=\"small-caps\">tinytim</span> (Krist <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib40\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib40\" data-open=\"bib40\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">1993</a>) are those used by the SLI reconstruction algorithm.</p><p class=\"chapter-para\">The left-hand column in Fig.\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-fig1\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"fig1\" data-open=\"fig1\" class=\"link link-reveal link-table xref-fig\">1</a> shows the resulting <em>F</em>110\u8201<em>W</em> and <em>F</em>160\u8201<em>W</em> images for each system. We applied annular elliptical masks (after PSF convolution), fitted by eye to each image, to include only the lensed image features. This also masks any noisy residuals which remain after the <span class=\"small-caps\">galfit</span> subtraction.</p>                        <a id=\"111789721\" scrollto-destination=\"111789721\"></a>\r\n<div data-id=\"fig1\" class=\"fig fig-section js-fig-section\"><div class=\"label fig-label\">Figure 1.</div><div class=\"graphic-wrap\"><img class=\"content-image\" src=\"https://oup.silverchair-cdn.com/oup/backfile/Content_public/Journal/mnras/440/3/10.1093_mnras_stu305/3/m_stu305fig1a.jpeg?Expires=1611467636&amp;Signature=d7FRtSvUv~2Ch~qwc4ycoR5AXsAeawNG9IxYzNZvidalJ4ZrhzsyYl0Ra0sIyKyIvlbhdxnKCM1ofXDu-b2H2AuF7l5jCwDz-v9fdt47J63wYS2kF8vI8vBKxHMxRX-GhgC8d2r~D1GRD6QKASRgXEj5bmLv7b~xVCj947taPs9e57DYSm0Upn4S7E-pHrtYq73tkmy~cnypSZzqIXD~Z2503UMCgVIdIVG1k4yeOIUCVKHmVEQRW5gO7CCI~dQUW7TUVKq3MyM-ZBMJEmQBZNzBOLi8km1x0NOrTSYs8yC0tUIHgkDNZFuiPcnY9he0QGduvwHm9cnPaRVm7RwM-g__&amp;Key-Pair-Id=APKAIE5G5CRDK6RD3PGA\" alt=\"Lens reconstructions. Reading from left to right, columns show the observed image (masked and lens subtracted), the model image, the residuals (observed image minus model; grey-scale same as corresponding images) and reconstructed source surface brightness map (the solid black or white line shows the caustic and the dashed white line and small circle, respectively, show the source half-light area and source centre obtained by B13 at 880\u00a0 $\\mu$ m). For each system, the F110\u8201W and F160\u8201W data are shown. In all panels, north points along the positive y-axis and west points along the positive x-axis.\" data-path-from-xml=\"stu305fig1a.jpeg\" /><div class=\"fig-orig original-slide\"><a class=\"fig-view-orig js-view-large openInAnotherWindow\" href=\"/view-large/figure/111789721/stu305fig1a.jpeg\" data-path-from-xml=\"stu305fig1a.jpeg\" target=\"_blank\">Open in new tab</a><a data-section=\"111789721\" href=\"/DownloadFile/DownloadImage.aspx?image=https://oup.silverchair-cdn.com/oup/backfile/Content_public/Journal/mnras/440/3/10.1093_mnras_stu305/3/stu305fig1a.jpeg?Expires=1611467636&Signature=3YDUl0KCnyvrjQFO0YTVnJZcMuy5SNanxxS6MWoI0Jw4X8eVU9grPL3X12J3GTLSZOmfHZ6rlKzJTXbA443aJ4nGUhQ-NhVdYu6mk51uhSIIJbL-JpM0G93TSoTNEGKX68~28ps~U-e70r-e1w960WnfrHFJPEtrXzxqw5bLCg9O2bdkpQhzdwdXN0pVaIh4tGexER-ziawsd~GwHgTqhNr7cjJvkhFIYaedOSNM7CpGBgw8ZYjtQEzK~8yamvcYjPRndO5FBlP0sYZqEqFnth7l0bZc3tddI5HaknVi-3cVCbV7Yp8lTUg0TSplvccQFGr4djOXkNRmVeL40X0HWg__&Key-Pair-Id=APKAIE5G5CRDK6RD3PGA&sec=111789721&ar=1074641&xsltPath=~/UI/app/XSLT&imagename=&siteId=5326\" class=\"download-slide\" data-path-from-xml=\"stu305fig1a.jpeg\">Download slide</a></div></div><div class=\"caption fig-caption\"><p class=\"chapter-para\">Lens reconstructions. Reading from left to right, columns show the observed image (masked and lens subtracted), the model image, the residuals (observed image minus model; grey-scale same as corresponding images) and reconstructed source surface brightness map (the solid black or white line shows the caustic and the dashed white line and small circle, respectively, show the source half-light area and source centre obtained by <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib14\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib14\" data-open=\"bib14\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">B13</a> at 880\u00a0| $\\mu$ |m). For each system, the <em>F</em>110\u8201<em>W</em> and <em>F</em>160\u8201<em>W</em> data are shown. In all panels, north points along the positive <em>y</em>-axis and west points along the positive <em>x</em>-axis.</p></div></div>                        <a id=\"111789722\" class=\"graphic-wrap\"><img class=\"content-image\" href=\"/DownloadFile/DownloadImage.aspx?image=https://oup.silverchair-cdn.com/oup/backfile/Content_public/Journal/mnras/440/3/10.1093_mnras_stu305/3/stu305fig1b.jpeg?Expires=1611467636&Signature=gmpexAq1zh5ENbpmsQKUFNerf~6YX9Zule~tb2zv3CvvnK2YrW5tqao02zeMQcmyfLRwk6KGHjgs67BDE~HIcXts20tSyRhJrT7LHAPkKvxbFGdCcMh~d5-lOLuFt9gT5zjk2TdWsEVlmo4o3N8s3AFCSd3ux2UBpATwouqVgvUs~xmVDwrD-prg7564UCXB-tpplD0d9t0H9~ovYs~3FAvTUXJSeuXL~zsI3cd06bgU6mPDaLxXw3tL-ILKGt8lfrCA8-gXxkWNfIMocpeyBW0iB7thDxVubi8Tu1Y3TEUChziMxV61U41Wl5b79iefXFjGqx2573m5MbX0ABgDIA__&Key-Pair-Id=APKAIE5G5CRDK6RD3PGA&sec=111789722&ar=1074641&xsltPath=~/UI/app/XSLT&imagename=&siteId=5326\" class=\"download-slide\" data-path-from-xml=\"stu305fig1b.jpeg\">Download slide</a></div></div><div class=\"caption fig-caption\"><p class=\"chapter-para\">\u2013\u2003<em>continued</em></p></div></div>                    <h2 scrollto-destination=111789723 id=\"111789723\" class=\"section-title\" data-legacy-id=sec3>3 METHODOLOGY</h2>\r\n<p class=\"chapter-para\">In this paper, we apply the SLI method originally derived by Warren &amp; Dye (<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib80\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib80\" data-open=\"bib80\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2003</a>). We use the Bayesian version of the SLI method applied by Dye et\u00a0al. (<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib27\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib27\" data-open=\"bib27\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2007</a>, <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib28\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib28\" data-open=\"bib28\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2008</a>), based on the version developed by Suyu et\u00a0al. (<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib64\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib64\" data-open=\"bib64\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2006</a>). In addition, the adaptive source plane grid introduced by Dye &amp; Warren (<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib26\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib26\" data-open=\"bib26\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2005</a>) is used.</p><p class=\"chapter-para\">In this section, we describe an enhancement to the SLI method that allows multiple images to be simultaneously reconstructed using the same lens mass model. Including multiple images in the inversion gives rise to stronger constraints on the lens model parameters. This is particularly true if the images are observed at different wavelengths since colour variations across the lensed source mean that each image probes a different line of sight through the gravitational potential of the lensing galaxy. We describe the modifications necessary for the inclusion of multiple images but refer the reader to the aforementioned papers for more comprehensive details of the underlying SLI method.</p>                    <h3 scrollto-destination=111789726 id=\"111789726\" class=\"section-title\" data-legacy-id=sec3-1>3.1 Multi-image SLI method</h3>\r\n<p class=\"chapter-para\">The SLI method assumes a pixelized image and a pixelized source plane. For a given lens model, the method computes the linear superposition of lensed images of each source plane pixel that best fits the observed lensed image. In the original formulation, the rectangular matrix <em>f</em><sub><em>ij</em></sub> held the fluxes of lensed image pixels <em>j</em> for each source plane pixel <em>i</em> of unit surface brightness. In this way, a model lensed image was created with flux values equal to \u2211<sub><em>i</em></sub>\u8201<em>s</em><sub><em>i</em></sub><em>f</em><sub><em>ij</em></sub> for each image pixel <em>j</em> given source pixel surface brightnesses <em>s</em><sub><em>i</em></sub>. Subtracting this model image from the observed image, which has pixel flux values <em>d</em><sub><em>j</em></sub> and 1\u03c3 uncertainties \u03c3<sub><em>j</em></sub>, allows the \u03c7<sup>2</sup> statistic to be computed.</p><div class=\"&#xA;            block-child-p&#xA;            \">To cope with multiple images, we need to introduce a new index to each of these quantities to denote separate image numbers. The \u03c7<sup>2</sup> statistic in this case becomes <div class=\"formula-wrap\"><div class=\"disp-formula\" id=\"equ1\" content-id=\"equ1\"><div class=\"tex-math display-math\"> \\begin{equation}\r\n\\chi ^2=\\sum _{k=1}^K \\left[ \\sum _{j=1}^{J_k} \\left( \\frac{\\sum _{i=1}^{I_k} s_i^k f_{ij}^k - d_j^k}{\\sigma _j^k}\\right)^2 \\right],\r\n\\end{equation} </div></div><span class=\"label\">(1)</span></div>where there is now an additional sum over images <em>k</em>. Note that each image <em>k</em> has its own source image with surface brightnesses | $s_i^k$ | in pixels <em>i</em>. The image of each of these pixels stored in | $f_{ij}^k$ | must be convolved with the PSF of the observed image <em>k</em>. Also note that each of the <em>k</em> sources and <em>k</em> images can have different numbers of pixels, <em>I</em><sub><em>k</em></sub> and <em>J</em><sub><em>k</em></sub>, respectively.</div><div class=\"&#xA;            block-child-p&#xA;            \">As in the single-image version of the SLI method, the minimum \u03c7<sup>2</sup> solution is given by <div class=\"formula-wrap\"><div class=\"disp-formula\" id=\"equ2\" content-id=\"equ2\"><div class=\"tex-math display-math\"> \\begin{equation}\r\n{\\boldsymbol {s}} = {\\bf F}^{-1} {\\boldsymbol d}\r\n\\end{equation} </div></div><span class=\"label\">(2)</span></div>but now the matrix | ${\\bf F}$ | is a block-diagonal matrix diag(| ${\\bf F}$ |<sup>1</sup>, | ${\\bf F}$ |<sup>2</sup>, \u2026, | ${\\bf F}$ |<sup><em>K</em></sup>) comprising the submatrices | ${\\bf F}$ |<sup><em>k</em></sup> and | ${\\boldsymbol d}$ | is a column vector which itself has column vector elements | ${\\boldsymbol d}^k$ |. The elements of | ${\\bf F}$ |<sup><em>k</em></sup> and | ${\\boldsymbol d}^k$ | are, respectively, <div class=\"formula-wrap\"><div class=\"disp-formula\" id=\"equ3\" content-id=\"equ3\"><div class=\"tex-math display-math\"> \\begin{equation}\r\nF_{ij}^{k}=\\sum _{n=1}^{J_n}\\,f_{\\rm in}^k f_{jn}^k / (\\sigma _n^k)^2 \\, \\,, \\quad d_i^k=\\sum _{n=1}^{J_n}\\,f_{\\rm in}^k d_{n}^k / (\\sigma _n^k)^2 \\,.\r\n\\end{equation} </div></div><span class=\"label\">(3)</span></div>Finally, the column vector | ${\\boldsymbol {s}}$ | contains the source pixel surface brightnesses arranged in order | $s_1^1, s_2^1,\\ldots , s_{I_1}^1, s_1^2,\\ldots , s_{I_K-1}^K, s_{I_K}^K$ |.</div><p class=\"chapter-para\">To regularize the solution, equation <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-equ2\"></span><a href=\"#equ2\" class=\"sectionLink xref-sec\">(2)</a> must be modified by the regularization matrix | ${\\bf H}$ | as described in Warren &amp; Dye (<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib80\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib80\" data-open=\"bib80\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2003</a>). However, in the case of multiple images, | ${\\bf H}$ | becomes the block diagonal matrix diag(\u03bb<sub>1</sub>| ${\\bf H}$ |<sup>1</sup>, \u03bb<sub>2</sub>| ${\\bf H}$ |<sup>2</sup>, \u2026, \u03bb<sub><em>K</em></sub>| ${\\bf H}$ |<sup><em>K</em></sup>), where each submatrix | ${\\bf H}$ |<sup><em>k</em></sup> corresponds to the chosen regularization matrix for source <em>k</em>. Note that in this formalism, each source <em>k</em> is assigned its own independent regularization weight \u03bb<sub><em>k</em></sub>. We regularize each source plane using the scheme described in Dye et\u00a0al. (<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib28\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib28\" data-open=\"bib28\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2008</a>) appropriate for adaptive source grids. In principle, instead of regularizing each source plane independently, different source planes could be allowed to regularize one another in which case | ${\\bf H}$ | would not be block-diagonal. This could be beneficial if the source is expected to be similar between the different input images but would bias the lens model if not.</p><div class=\"&#xA;            block-child-p&#xA;            \">The procedure for finding the most probable lens model parameters then turns to Bayesian inference as described by Dye et\u00a0al. (<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib28\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib28\" data-open=\"bib28\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2008</a>). Adapting equation 7 in Dye et\u00a0al. (<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib28\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib28\" data-open=\"bib28\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2008</a>) to the multi-image case here results in the following expression for the Bayesian evidence, \u03f5: <div class=\"formula-wrap\"><div class=\"disp-formula\" id=\"equ4\" content-id=\"equ4\"><div class=\"tex-math display-math\"> \\begin{eqnarray}\r\n-2 \\,{\\rm ln} \\, \\epsilon &amp;=&amp; \\chi ^2 +{\\rm ln} \\, \\left[ {\\rm det} (\\bf F+\\bf H)\\right] -{\\rm ln} \\, \\left[ {\\rm det} (\\bf H)\\right] \\nonumber \\\\\r\n&amp; &amp; +\\, \\boldsymbol {s}^{{\\rm T}}{\\bf H}\\boldsymbol {s} + \\sum _{k=1}^K\\sum _{j=1}^{J_k} {\\rm ln} \\left[2\\pi (\\sigma _j^k)^2 \\right] \\,\r\n\\end{eqnarray} </div></div><span class=\"label\">(4)</span></div>with \u03c7<sup>2</sup> given by equation <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-equ1\"></span><a href=\"#equ1\" class=\"sectionLink xref-sec\">(1)</a> and where | ${\\bf F}$ |, | ${\\bf H}$ | and | ${\\boldsymbol {s}}$ | are the multi-image quantities defined above. The negative logarithm of the evidence as given above is then minimized by applying the Markov Chain Monte Carlo (MCMC) technique to the lens model parameters (see the next section), the regularization weights<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-fn2\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"fn2\" data-open=\"fn2\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-fn js-xref-fn\"><sup>1</sup></a> and a parameter called the \u2018splitting factor\u2019 which controls the distribution of source plane pixel sizes on the adaptive grid (see Dye et\u00a0al. <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib28\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib28\" data-open=\"bib28\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2008</a>, for more details). After the MCMC chain has burnt in, we allow a further 100\u00a0000 iterations to estimate parameter confidences.</div><p class=\"chapter-para\">We note a further practicality. When computing the \u03c7<sup>2</sup> term in equation <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-equ4\"></span><a href=\"#equ4\" class=\"sectionLink xref-sec\">(4)</a>, we carry out the sum over image pixels contained within an annular mask surrounding the ring. The mask is tailored for each image to include the image of the entire source plane, with minimal extraneous sky. This increases the fraction of significant image pixels with the effect that the evidence is more sensitive to the model parameters.</p><p class=\"chapter-para\">Finally, we point out that the multi-image SLI method as presented assumes that all images are statistically independent of each other. In the case of images that are not statistically independent, for example, as could be the case with image slices in an integral field unit data cube or spectral-line interferometric data cube, equation <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-equ1\"></span><a href=\"#equ1\" class=\"sectionLink xref-sec\">(1)</a> must be modified to include the relevant covariance terms.</p>                    <h3 scrollto-destination=111789734 id=\"111789734\" class=\"section-title\" data-legacy-id=sec3-2>3.2 Lens model</h3>\r\n<p class=\"chapter-para\">We model each of the five lenses considered in this work with a single smooth density profile to describe the distribution of the total (baryonic and dark) lens mass. In order to directly compare with the work of Bolton et\u00a0al. (<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib9\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib9\" data-open=\"bib9\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2012</a>) and Sonnenfeld et\u00a0al. (<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib62\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib62\" data-open=\"bib62\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2013</a>), we use the power-law density profile assumed in these studies. The volume mass density of this profile, \u03c1, scales with radius, <em>r</em>, as \u03c1\u00a0\u221d\u00a0<em>r</em><sup>\u2212\u03b1</sup>. The implicit assumption made with this profile is that the power-law slope, \u03b1, is scale invariant. This assumption appears to be reasonable, at least on the scales probed by strong lensing, since there is no apparent trend in slope with the ratio of Einstein radius to effective radius (Koopmans et\u00a0al. <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib39\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib39\" data-open=\"bib39\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2006</a>; Ruff et\u00a0al. <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib57\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib57\" data-open=\"bib57\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2011</a>).</p><div class=\"&#xA;            block-child-p&#xA;            \">The corresponding projected mass density profile we therefore use in the lens modelling is the elliptical power-law profile introduced by Kassiola &amp; Kovner (<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib37\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib37\" data-open=\"bib37\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">1993</a>), which has a surface mass density, \u03ba, given by <div class=\"formula-wrap\"><div class=\"disp-formula\" id=\"equ5\" content-id=\"equ5\"><div class=\"tex-math display-math\"> \\begin{equation}\r\n\\kappa =\\kappa _0\\,({\\tilde{r}}/{\\rm 1\\,kpc})^{1-\\alpha } \\,,\r\n\\end{equation} </div></div><span class=\"label\">(5)</span></div>where \u03ba<sub>0</sub> is the normalization surface mass density (the special case of \u03b1\u00a0=\u00a02 corresponds to the singular isothermal ellipsoid). The radius | ${\\tilde{r}}$ | is the elliptical radius defined by | ${\\tilde{r}}^2 =x^{\\prime 2}+y^{\\prime 2}/\\epsilon ^2$ |, where \u03f5 is the lens elongation defined as the ratio of the semimajor to semiminor axes. There are three further parameters that describe this profile: the orientation of the semimajor axis measured in a counterclockwise sense from north, \u03b8, and the coordinates of the centre of the lens in the image plane, (<em>x</em><sub>c</sub>, <em>y</em><sub>c</sub>). We also include two further parameters to allow for an external shear field, namely, the shear strength, \u03b3, and shear direction angle, again measured counterclockwise from north, \u03b8<sub>\u03b3</sub>. The shear direction angle is defined to be perpendicular to the direction of resulting image stretch. This brings the total number of lens model parameters to eight. We assume a uniform prior for all eight parameters. In the MCMC contour plots presented in the appendix, we marginalize over (<em>x</em><sub>c</sub>, <em>y</em><sub>c</sub>) since we did not detect any significant offsets between the lens mass centre and the centroid of the lens galaxy light.</div>                    <h2 scrollto-destination=111789737 id=\"111789737\" class=\"section-title\" data-legacy-id=sec4>4 RESULTS</h2>\r\n<div class=\"&#xA;            block-child-p&#xA;            \">The reconstruction of each of the five lenses is shown in Fig.\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-fig1\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"fig1\" data-open=\"fig1\" class=\"link link-reveal link-table xref-fig\">1</a>. Table\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-tbl1\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"tbl1\" data-open=\"tbl1\" class=\"link link-reveal link-table xref-fig\">1</a> gives the lens model parameters, including the geometric average Einstein radius, \u03b8<sub>E</sub>, computed as <div class=\"formula-wrap\"><div class=\"disp-formula\" id=\"equ6\" content-id=\"equ6\"><div class=\"tex-math display-math\"> \\begin{equation}\r\n\\left(\\frac{\\theta _{\\rm E}}{1\\,\\rm kpc}\\right)= \\left(\\frac{2}{3-\\alpha } \\, \\frac{1}{\\sqrt{\\epsilon }} \\, \\frac{\\kappa _o}{\\Sigma _{\\rm CR}}\\right)^{\\frac{1}{\\alpha -1}},\r\n\\end{equation} </div></div><span class=\"label\">(6)</span></div>where \u03a3<sub>CR</sub> is the critical surface mass density (see, for example Schneider, Ehlers &amp; Falco <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib60\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib60\" data-open=\"bib60\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">1992</a>).</div>                        <a id=\"111789739\" scrollto-destination=\"111789739\"></a>\r\n<div content-id=\"tbl1\" class=\"table-modal table-full-width-wrap\"><div class=\"table-wrap table-wide\"><div class=\"table-wrap-title\" id=\"tbl1\" data-id=\"tbl1\"><span class=\"label\">Table 1.</span><div class=\"caption\"><p class=\"chapter-para\">Lens model parameters. Reading from left to right, columns are the H-ATLAS identifier (including the Negrello et\u00a0al. <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib48\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib48\" data-open=\"bib48\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2010</a> identifier), the lens redshift, | $z$ |<sub>d</sub>, the source redshift, | $z$ |<sub>s</sub>, the density profile slope, \u03b1, the lens mass normalization, \u03ba<sub>0</sub> (in units of 10<sup>10</sup>\u00a0M<sub>\u2299</sub>\u00a0kpc<sup>\u22122</sup>), the elongation of the lens mass profile, \u03f5, the orientation of the semimajor axis of the lens, \u03b8, measured counterclockwise from north, the strength of the external shear component, \u03b3, and the Einstein radius, \u03b8<sub>E</sub>, in href=\"/view-large/111789739\">\r\n          Open in new tab\r\n        </a></div></div></div>                    <h3 scrollto-destination=111789740 id=\"111789740\" class=\"section-title\" data-legacy-id=sec4-1>4.1 Object notes</h3>\r\n<p class=\"chapter-para\">In this section, we detail the characteristics of each lens system. In particular, we compare with the results of Bussmann et\u00a0al. (<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib14\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib14\" data-open=\"bib14\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2013</a>, <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib14\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib14\" data-open=\"bib14\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">B13</a> hereafter) who have modelled imaging data acquired with the Submillimeter Array (SMA) for \u223c30 candidate lenses discovered by H-ATLAS and HerMES. All five of the H-ATLAS SDP lenses are modelled by <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib14\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib14\" data-open=\"bib14\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">B13</a>, although we point out that external shear is not included in their lens model.</p><p class=\"chapter-para\"><em>J090311.6+003906</em> (ID81). This is a classic cusp\u2013caustic configuration lens. The near-IR emission in the lensed image closely matches that of the SMA data and unsurprisingly results in a consistent magnification. The reconstructed source in both <em>HST</em> filters shows little structure other than a slight elongation along the NE\u2013SW direction. The centroid of the source is well aligned with that found by <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib14\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib14\" data-open=\"bib14\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">B13</a>. The lens model is an excellent fit to the observed data and leaves no significant residuals. The model requires a small amount of external shear with strength \u03b3 \u2243 0.05 and direction \u03b8<sub>\u03b3</sub> \u2243 105\u00b0, consistent with perturbations expected from a nearby group of galaxies to the east. The elongation and orientation of the required model is entirely consistent with that of the observed light profile.</p><p class=\"chapter-para\"><em>J090740.0\u2212004200</em> (ID9). This lens system is dominated by a single doubly imaged source with a simple morphology lying to the north of the lens galaxy centroid. The double imaging is consistent with the SMA data but the emission appears to originate from a different location in the source compared to what is observed in the near-IR <em>HST</em> data. The reconstructed source <em>F</em>110\u8201<em>W</em>\u2013<em>F</em>160\u8201<em>W</em> colour map shows a reddening gradient which points from the near-IR source towards the SMA source centroid. Some of the fainter emission from the source in the near-IR crosses the caustic and gives rise to the observed complete Einstein ring. There is also some fainter structure in the ring which is fit in the model by a fainter source to the east of the lens centroid. Negligible external shear is required in the best-fitting lens model. The modest elongation of \u03f5\u00a0=\u00a01.14 indicates a more radially symmetric mass profile compared to the light. The mass and light are significantly misaligned in this lens (see Section\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-sec4-4\"></span><a href=\"#sec4-4\" class=\"sectionLink xref-sec\">4.4</a>).</p><p class=\"chapter-para\"><em>J091043.1\u2212000321</em> (ID11). Like ID9, this system has a complete Einstein ring. The ring's significant ellipticity is the result of a relatively strong external shear field of strength \u03b3\u00a0=\u00a00.23. The direction of this shear points almost exactly to the centre of a nearby edge-on spiral galaxy to the NW located at a redshift of | $z$ |\u00a0=\u00a00.39\u00a0\u00b1\u00a00.09 (see <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib49a\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib49a\" data-open=\"bib49a\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">N14</a>). The implication is therefore that this spiral is almost entirely responsible for the shear perturbation. We attempted a model where external shear was replaced by a second singular isothermal lens to represent the spiral's total mass but found no significant improvement in the fit. The reconstructed source exhibits clear small-scale structure in both filters, required to fit the observed structure in the ring. The majority of the emission observed in the ring comes from a doubly imaged source lying just outside the caustic and the smaller scale structure comes from smaller knots of emission in the source, some of which are quadruply imaged. As the residual plot in Fig.\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-fig1\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"fig1\" data-open=\"fig1\" class=\"link link-reveal link-table xref-fig\">1</a> indicates, the model image does not perfectly account for the observed features in the ring and this is also reflected in the fact that the model fit is only marginally acceptable. It is therefore possible that some of the ring structure is actually structure in the lens galaxy not fully removed by <span class=\"small-caps\">galfit</span>. The SMA data imply a doubly imaged source which, like J090740.0\u2212004200, is offset from the near-IR emission but has a comparable Einstein radius. As with ID9, the reconstructed source <em>F</em>110\u8201<em>W</em>\u2013<em>F</em>160\u8201<em>W</em> colour map shows a reddening gradient which points from the near-IR source towards the SMA source centroid.</p><p class=\"chapter-para\">In addition to the reconstructed near-IR source presented here, there appears to be further lensed arcs associated with this system (not shown in Fig.\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-fig1\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"fig1\" data-open=\"fig1\" class=\"link link-reveal link-table xref-fig\">1</a>). Two readily identified arcs lie to the south and to the east of the lens centre and trace out a ring with an Einstein radius larger than that shown in Fig.\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-fig1\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"fig1\" data-open=\"fig1\" class=\"link link-reveal link-table xref-fig\">1</a>. This implies that the lensed source responsible lies at a different redshift. We chose not to complicate the lens model further by incorporating this additional source but instead leave this for further work.</p><p class=\"chapter-para\"><em>J091305.0\u2212005343</em> (ID130). The lens galaxy is relatively poorly constrained in this system. The best-fitting model is consistent with zero external shear (as might be expected from the lack of observed nearby perturbers) and the lens has one of the higher elongations found in the sample of \u03f5\u00a0=\u00a01.34. The best-fitting reconstructed source is very extended and as such, the magnification is low. The residual map shown in Fig.\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-fig1\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"fig1\" data-open=\"fig1\" class=\"link link-reveal link-table xref-fig\">1</a> shows some significant features towards the lens centre which is not surprising given the difficulty reported by <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib49a\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib49a\" data-open=\"bib49a\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">N14</a> in removing the lens light. However, the residuals contribute an insignificant amount of light to the overall source and as such will have a negligible effect on the lens model parameters. The SMA data are in close agreement with the near-IR data which increases confidence in the <span class=\"small-caps\">galfit</span> subtraction (see <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib49a\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib49a\" data-open=\"bib49a\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">N14</a>), although the near-IR data imply a slightly larger magnification than the SMA data. We make the caveat that this lens, as pointed out by <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib49a\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib49a\" data-open=\"bib49a\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">N14</a>, is a likely Sa galaxy. In order to compare our results with those of the aforementioned lensing studies which include only early-type lenses, we have omitted this system from our fits to the trends reported later in this paper.</p><p class=\"chapter-para\"><em>J090302.9\u2212014127</em> (ID17). This is a relatively poorly constrained lens although the fit is acceptable. There are some minor features in the residual image which occur at 3 o'clock and 11 o'clock around the inner radius of the annulus as shown in Fig.\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-fig1\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"fig1\" data-open=\"fig1\" class=\"link link-reveal link-table xref-fig\">1</a>. <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib49a\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib49a\" data-open=\"bib49a\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">N14</a> have modelled the flux in the lensed image by fitting individual <span class=\"small-caps\">galfit</span> profiles to the different components. Two of these profiles lie on the inner radius of the annulus and these coincide with the locations of the residuals found in the lens modelling here. One interpretation is therefore that spatially dependent extinction in the lensing galaxy affects the lensed image. This is consistent with the colour of the feature at 11 o'clock which <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib49a\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib49a\" data-open=\"bib49a\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">N14</a> determine as having a significantly redder colour than the average colour of the other <span class=\"small-caps\">galfit</span> profiles, but not with the feature at 3 o'clock which is consistent in colour. An alternative explanation might therefore be that the lensed image contains residual flux from the lensing galaxy itself which is highly possible given the complexity in removing the lens from this system. Nevertheless, the reconstructed source plane shows two very prominent elongated objects in both bands which converge at a point interior to the caustic. The majority of this source plane emission is doubly imaged but some of the flux in the merged region is quadruply imaged. The fact that the lensed image is well fitted by a relatively simple source surface brightness map adds reassurance that the reconstruction is plausible; an over-complicated source often indicates that there are non-lensed features in the lensed image. Although the SMA data for this lens are unable to resolve individual ring features, <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib14\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib14\" data-open=\"bib14\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">B13</a> obtain a magnification consistent with that measured from the near-IR data here.</p>                    <h3 scrollto-destination=111789748 id=\"111789748\" class=\"section-title\" data-legacy-id=sec4-2>4.2 Mass profile versus redshift</h3>\r\n<p class=\"chapter-para\">Fig.\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-fig2\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"fig2\" data-open=\"fig2\" class=\"link link-reveal link-table xref-fig\">2</a> shows the fitted lens density profile slopes plotted against redshift. Discounting the likely Sa lens J091305.0\u2212005343, the straight line minimum \u03c7<sup>2</sup> fit through the four data points is \u03b1\u00a0=\u00a02.05 \u00b1 0.08\u00a0\u2212\u00a0(0.30 \u00b1 0.13)| $z$ |. (If we include this fifth lens, the fit becomes \u03b1\u00a0=\u00a02.01 \u00b1 0.10\u00a0\u2212\u00a0(0.25 \u00b1 0.15)| $z$ |.) The dotted line and grey shaded envelope in Fig.\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-fig2\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"fig2\" data-open=\"fig2\" class=\"link link-reveal link-table xref-fig\">2</a> shows the fit and the 1\u03c3 error region, respectively. In the same figure, we plot the variation in slope with redshift from three other lens sample combinations: (1) the SL2S lens sample of \u03b1\u00a0=\u00a02.05 \u00b1 0.06\u00a0\u2212\u00a0(0.13 \u00b1 0.24)| $z$ | from Sonnenfeld et\u00a0al. (<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib62\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib62\" data-open=\"bib62\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2013</a>), (2) the combination of the Lensing Structure and Dynamics sample of Treu &amp; Koopmans (<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib69\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib69\" data-open=\"bib69\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2004</a>), SL2S and SLACS of \u03b1\u00a0=\u00a02.08 \u00b1 0.02\u00a0\u2212\u00a0(0.31 \u00b1 0.10)| $z$ | also from Sonnenfeld et\u00a0al. (<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib62\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib62\" data-open=\"bib62\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2013</a>) and (3) the combination of SLACS and BOSS of \u03b1\u00a0= 2.11 \u00b1 0.02\u00a0\u2212\u00a0(0.60 \u00b1 0.15)| $z$ | from Bolton et\u00a0al. (<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib9\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib9\" data-open=\"bib9\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2012</a>).</p>                        <a id=\"111789750\" scrollto-destination=\"111789750\"></a>\r\n<div data-id=\"fig2\" class=\"fig fig-section js-fig-section\"><div class=\"label fig-label\">Figure 2.</div><div class=\"graphic-wrap\"><img class=\"content-image\" src=\"https://oup.silverchair-cdn.com/oup/backfile/Content_public/Journal/mnras/440/3/10.1093_mnras_stu305/3/m_stu305fig2.jpeg?Expires=1611467636&amp;Signature=omP9lLobm1JkuOVFvEqhkfe~gdYtR1YNn-7w3MZTrn6DhZVyddGLS0ij37RGtv~AtWSM2H2FTwpKPjdvoIiu0WzLwm66W3Npn3VCxeSkAtKIjXuYV86H9Zj1UZgX0isjsCPU6XoldAzFz44CpOhNMExFNZupgg5rkhZZu7b6NW5q6DqSgiC2j21SRNlEcy~YfSOojaVopCNNFrcbi642Z9XzkxsoxK4LXHh1VkajrSJLk9qzukrT7ZJfFNjVtGxb8tyh0-rG5uYvgqW6mK6koWK1o~RKi7-vKVbDKz0INcP194j2fzQld1jO2wHDl6VSotIxfc8RtV5dJ53EDGT5IQ__&amp;Key-Pair-Id=APKAIE5G5CRDK6RD3PGA\" alt=\"Variation of the density profile slope, \u03b1, as a function of the lens redshift,  $z$ d. This is perhaps not surprising given the small sample size presently at our disposal. This limitation will be considerably reduced by our forthcoming rapidly growing lens sample.</p>                    <h3 scrollto-destination=111789752 id=\"111789752\" class=\"section-title\" data-legacy-id=sec4-3>4.3 Lens magnifications</h3>\r\n<p class=\"chapter-para\">Table\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-tbl2\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"tbl2\" data-open=\"tbl2\" class=\"link link-reveal link-table xref-fig\">2</a> lists the source flux magnifications. For each lens, we have computed the magnification at every point in the MCMC chain as presented in Fig.\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-fig6\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"fig6\" data-open=\"fig6\" class=\"link link-reveal link-table xref-fig\">A1</a> to form a magnification distribution. Table\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-tbl2\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"tbl2\" data-open=\"tbl2\" class=\"link link-reveal link-table xref-fig\">2</a> then quotes the median magnification and its \u00b134\u00a0per\u8201cent bounds.</p>                        <a id=\"111789754\" scrollto-destination=\"111789754\"></a>\r\n<div content-id=\"tbl2\" class=\"table-modal table-full-width-wrap\"><div class=\"table-wrap table-wide\"><div class=\"table-wrap-title\" id=\"tbl2\" data-id=\"tbl2\"><span class=\"label\">Table 2.</span><div class=\"caption\"><p class=\"chapter-para\">Source flux magnifications. |\u00a0</td><td>| $4.48^{+0.33}_{-0.25}$ |\u00a0</td><td>| $5.54^{+0.41}_{-0.30}$ |\u00a0</td><td>4.9 \u00b1 0.7\u00a0</td></tr></tbody></table></div><div class=\"&#xA;          graphic-wrap&#xA;          \"><a class=\"fig-view-orig openInAnotherWindow btn js-view-large\" target=\"_blank\" href=\"/view-large/111789754\">\r\n          Open in new tab\r\n        </a></div></div></div><p class=\"chapter-para\">We computed magnifications using the higher signal-to-noise <em>F</em>160\u8201<em>W</em> data to give more precise magnifications. We find that the magnifications computed using the <em>F</em>110\u8201<em>W</em> data generally have a larger spread but the distribution is always consistent with those computed using the <em>F</em>160\u8201<em>W</em> data.</p><p class=\"chapter-para\">We determined three different magnifications to give an indication of the strength of near-IR differential amplification. The first is a \u2018total magnification\u2019, \u03bc<sub>tot</sub>, computed as the ratio of the total flux in the masked region of the image as shown in Fig.\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-fig1\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"fig1\" data-open=\"fig1\" class=\"link link-reveal link-table xref-fig\">1</a> to the total flux in the source plane. The second and third, \u03bc<sub>0.5</sub> and \u03bc<sub>0.1</sub>, correspond to the magnification of the brightest region(s) of the source that contributes 50 and 10\u00a0per\u8201cent, respectively, of the total reconstructed source flux. Note that \u03bc<sub>tot</sub> is almost always lower than \u03bc<sub>0.5</sub> and \u03bc<sub>0.1</sub> since incorporating the total source plane typically includes additional regions that are less magnified.</p><p class=\"chapter-para\">Table\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-tbl2\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"tbl2\" data-open=\"tbl2\" class=\"link link-reveal link-table xref-fig\">2</a> also lists the magnifications, \u03bc<sub>880</sub>, determined by <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib14\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib14\" data-open=\"bib14\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">B13</a> at 880\u00a0| $\\mu$ |m. These magnifications are calculated in an elliptical disc centred on the best-fitting 880\u00a0| $\\mu$ |m source brightness profile with a radius twice that containing half of the total source flux. \u03bc<sub>880</sub> is the ratio of the integrated flux within the image plane region mapped by this disc to the integrated flux within the disc in the source plane itself. For the S\u00e9rsic profiles fit to the SDP sources by <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib14\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib14\" data-open=\"bib14\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">B13</a>, this corresponds to approximately 75\u00a0per\u8201cent of the source light in all cases. Therefore, \u03bc<sub>880</sub> corresponds to a magnification somewhere between \u03bc<sub>tot</sub> and \u03bc<sub>0.5</sub>.</p><p class=\"chapter-para\">As Table\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-tbl2\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"tbl2\" data-open=\"tbl2\" class=\"link link-reveal link-table xref-fig\">2</a> shows, for all lenses apart from J091305.0\u2212005343, \u03bc<sub>880</sub> is consistent with a value spanned by \u03bc<sub>tot</sub> and \u03bc<sub>0.5</sub>. The consistency is generally better when the 880| $\\,\\mu$ |m source morphology more closely resembles the reconstructed near-IR source. This is a reflection of the fact that the lens models determined at both wavelengths are geometrically similar.<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-fn3\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"fn3\" data-open=\"fn3\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-fn js-xref-fn\"><sup>2</sup></a> The exception is J091305.0\u2212005343 which has a very similar source morphology between 880| $\\,\\mu$ |m and the near-IR, but in this case, the magnification discrepancy is brought about by a significantly different lens elongation, with the near-IR lens model favouring an elongation of \u223c1.3 compared to \u223c2.0 at 880| $\\,\\mu$ |m.</p>                    <h3 scrollto-destination=111789759 id=\"111789759\" class=\"section-title\" data-legacy-id=sec4-4>4.4 Comparison of mass and light morphology</h3>\r\n<p class=\"chapter-para\">A question which brings insight to models of galaxy formation and evolution is how closely the visible mass traces the dark matter halo. One way to address this is to compare the visible morphology of the lens galaxies to the total mass profiles determined through lensing.</p><p class=\"chapter-para\">We used the <span class=\"small-caps\">galfit</span> models of <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib49a\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib49a\" data-open=\"bib49a\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">N14</a> to determine the elongation and orientation of the lens galaxy surface brightness profiles. Table\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-tbl3\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"tbl3\" data-open=\"tbl3\" class=\"link link-reveal link-table xref-fig\">3</a> lists these along with the effective radii. In the case of J091305.0\u2212005343 (ID130), we used only the light profiles which make up the bulge, since the bulge comprises nearly all of the light and, unlike the faint disc, has a coherent set of profiles which give a well-defined orientation and elongation.</p>                        <a id=\"111789762\" scrollto-destination=\"111789762\"></a>\r\n<div content-id=\"tbl3\" class=\"table-modal table-full-width-wrap\"><div class=\"table-wrap table-wide\"><div class=\"table-wrap-title\" id=\"tbl3\" data-id=\"tbl3\"><span class=\"label\">Table 3.</span><div class=\"caption\"><p class=\"chapter-para\">Morphology of the lens light profile and lens masses. Columns are: elongation, \u03f5<sub>light</sub>, position angle (measured counterclockwise from north), \u03b8<sub>light</sub>, effective radius, <em>R</em><sub>e</sub>, the stellar mass contained within a radius of <em>R</em><sub>e</sub>/2 for a Salpeter and Chabrier IMF, | $M^{\\rm *,Salp}_{R_{\\rm e}/2}$ | and | $M^{\\rm *,Chab}_{R_{\\rm e}/2}$ |, respectively (derived from the stellar masses computed in <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib49a\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib49a\" data-open=\"bib49a\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">N14</a>) and the total mass within a radius of <em>R</em><sub>e</sub>/2 inferred from the lens model, | $M^{\\rm Tot}_{R_{\\rm e}/2}$ |. All masses are in units of 10<sup>10</sup>\u8201M<sub>\u2299</sub>. Note that the light profile parameters for J091305.0\u2212005343 refer only to the bulge component and exclude the faint disc.</p></div> </div><div parameters. The top panel shows the comparison of elongations. For all five of the lenses, the total lens mass model has or is consistent with a lower elongation than that of the light. This implies that the dark matter halo is comparable in elongation or rounder in each case.</p>                        <a id=\"111789764\" scrollto-destination=\"111789764\"></a>\r\n<div data-id=\"fig3\" class=\"fig fig-section js-fig-section\"><div class=\"label fig-label\">Figure 3.</div><div class=\"graphic-wrap\"><img class=\"content-image\" src=\"https://oup.silverchair-cdn.com/oup/backfile/Content_public/Journal/mnras/440/3/10.1093_mnras_stu305/3/m_stu305fig3.jpeg?Expires=1611467636&amp;Signature=S3gIEny0lDOL684Jsm12qKOOk3tw9SKGb7QFAXlT4havc4KL93ZcHHyiEhlHjurzHb-9WX8M0~vrSP2krwPUBhM~Dx9uIzOvba2ATULGSftLYwhpwx3UGBHl3rSjxAZqiaEPQOrsPtKrWuxRNxzZ6bjI~NcRrnZ7LVGaBTvMRapZkyidTrKLFT3W9dX3aVkkhSzAnOUHp6q38iwG9KKbnoocY2VIqY8mM6~-sm1eQKK-PFUJTNo5Z038EDeFE6CeDoPEynjPycLpQ3JPhRwsJChqpv-3b09lxIqGaJB5mWKhsBndDv0fVHRqWM5fKW0bbCZpHafXjaq-irOxXWOWmA__&amp;Key-Pair-Id=APKAIE5G5CRDK6RD3PGA\" alt=\"Comparison of light with mass.
