Top panel: elongation of lens mass profile versus elongation of observed light. Bottom panel: difference in position angle of lens profile and observed light versus elongation of observed light. In both panels, the grey data point corresponds to the Sa lens J091305.0\u2212005343.\" data-path-from-xml=\"stu305fig3.jpeg\" /><div class=\"fig-orig original-slide\"><a class=\"fig-view-orig js-view-large openInAnotherWindow\" href=\"/view-large/figure/111789764/stu305fig3.jpeg\" data-path-from-xml=\"stu305fig3.jpeg\" target=\"_blank\">Open in new tab</a><a data-section=\"111789764\" href=\"/DownloadFile/DownloadImage.aspx?image=https://oup.silverchair-cdn.com/oup/backfile/Content_public/Journal/mnras/440/3/10.1093_mnras_stu305/3/stu305fig3.jpeg?Expires=1611467636&Signature=H1y8X1DtWlDzANnzmLIzkhGBlhBzKTbY~D9ilGUztlAuFnYqBAXoVMnWWJzdIO5HfzNV3MN3T2dXazh2PRt1beKNBceoTAhwgeAqKkzdpzMMaGCOFS2oWLPiVsA4gdhkwPefdtZ4X1OP1Ir9ajKDF8DfftQeSct0C~5EvGbbVK2Ios79cPRpGETaloVv7ZtgpDIb3hhR0L5qiJvV842XwjqE3ZU0XW-JryiG2xjPqBrrV2lv3YVzI-Bfzf9mW9wk-m7q7pXKq13wS9mdvYtXEo7O8g~yqDJC4oz8bB5iTMSrfg3tkoloHyuSzo622nMBT1h4jTbsh-2Sys16BuJGlw__&Key-Pair-Id=APKAIE5G5CRDK6RD3PGA&sec=111789764&ar=1074641&xsltPath=~/UI/app/XSLT&imagename=&siteId=5326\" class=\"download-slide\" data-path-from-xml=\"stu305fig3.jpeg\">Download slide</a></div></div><div class=\"caption fig-caption\"><p class=\"chapter-para\">Comparison of light with mass. <em>Top panel</em>: elongation of lens mass profile versus elongation of observed light. <em>Bottom panel</em>: difference in position angle of lens profile and observed light versus elongation of observed light. In both panels, the grey data point corresponds to the Sa lens J091305.0\u2212005343.</p></div></div><p class=\"chapter-para\">The bottom panel of Fig.\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-fig3\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"fig3\" data-open=\"fig3\" class=\"link link-reveal link-table xref-fig\">3</a> compares the offset in orientation of the lens mass and light profiles. Here, there are some significant discrepancies. The offsets are substantially higher on average than those found by SLACS, who measured an rms scatter of 10\u00b0 (Koopmans et\u00a0al. <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib39\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib39\" data-open=\"bib39\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2006</a>), but consistent with the findings of SL2S (Gavazzi et\u00a0al. <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib31\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib31\" data-open=\"bib31\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2012</a>) who measure an rms scatter of 25\u00b0 with offsets of up to 50\u00b0. As Gavazzi et\u00a0al. (<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib31\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib31\" data-open=\"bib31\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2012</a>) point out, the SL2S lenses have a higher average ratio of Einstein radius to effective radius than the SLACS lenses, and hence, the SLACS lenses are more dominated by the stellar component. The average of this ratio for our lenses is even higher than that of the SL2S sample and so we would expect even less alignment between the dark and visible components. We will be able to explore this trend more properly with our forthcoming larger lens sample, although there are already indications from simulations that such large (and even larger) morphological differences between baryons and dark matter are commonplace (for example, see Bett et\u00a0al. <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib6\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib6\" data-open=\"bib6\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2010</a>; Skibba &amp; Macci\u00f2 <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib61\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib61\" data-open=\"bib61\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2011</a>).</p>                    <h3 scrollto-destination=111789766 id=\"111789766\" class=\"section-title\" data-legacy-id=sec4-5>4.5 Other observational trends</h3>\r\n<p class=\"chapter-para\">An important observational benchmark for models of galaxy and structure formation is the fraction of dark matter contained within a fixed fraction of the effective radius. Using the SLACS lens sample, Auger et\u00a0al. (<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib3\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib3\" data-open=\"bib3\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2010b</a>) measure an average projected fraction of dark matter within half the effective radius of 0.21 with a scatter of 0.20, for a Salpeter (<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib58\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib58\" data-open=\"bib58\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">1955</a>) IMF, or 0.55 with a scatter of 0.11 for a Chabrier (<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib17\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib17\" data-open=\"bib17\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2003</a>) IMF. Ruff et\u00a0al. (<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib57\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib57\" data-open=\"bib57\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2011</a>) measure the average of this fraction to be 0.42 with a scatter of 0.20 for a Salpeter IMF for the SL2S lenses.</p><p class=\"chapter-para\">Table\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-tbl3\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"tbl3\" data-open=\"tbl3\" class=\"link link-reveal link-table xref-fig\">3</a> lists the stellar masses and the total lensing projected mass contained within half the effective radius for our lenses. To obtain these stellar masses, we used the total stellar masses and the <span class=\"small-caps\">galfit</span> profiles determined for the lens galaxies by <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib49a\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib49a\" data-open=\"bib49a\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">N14</a>. Excluding the Sa lens J091305.0\u2212005343, our lenses have a mean projected dark matter fraction within half the effective radius of <em>f</em><sub>DM</sub>\u00a0=\u00a00.46 with a scatter of 0.10, for a Salpeter IMF, (<em>f</em><sub>DM</sub>\u00a0=\u00a00.69 with a scatter of 0.07, for a Chabrier IMF). Although this is statistically consistent with the SLACS and SL2S lenses, the higher value measured in both our lenses and the SL2S lenses compared to the SLACS sample most likely reflects the lower average ratio of Einstein radius to effective radius in SLACS.</p><p class=\"chapter-para\">One of the trends detected by the SLACS survey is that <em>f</em><sub>DM</sub> for early types increases with galaxy mass and galaxy size. Auger et\u00a0al. (<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib3\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib3\" data-open=\"bib3\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2010b</a>) measure the linear fit | $f_{\\rm DM}=-0.13\\pm 0.09 + (0.49\\pm 0.10)\\log ({R_{\\rm e}/1\\,{\\rm kpc}})$ |. However, this result is not confirmed by the SL2S lens sample; Ruff et\u00a0al. (<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib57\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib57\" data-open=\"bib57\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2011</a>) fail to find any correlation between <em>f</em><sub>DM</sub> and | $R_{\\rm e}$ | with a measured gradient of | ${\\rm d}f_{\\rm DM}/{{\\rm d}R_{\\rm e}}=0.08^{+0.10}_{-0.08}$ |\u00a0kpc<sup>\u22121</sup>. Our lens sample, excluding the Sa lens J091305.0\u2212005343 gives a linear fit of | $f_{\\rm DM}=-0.01\\pm 0.20 + (1.04\\pm 0.46)\\log ({R_{\\rm e}/1\\,{\\rm kpc}})$ |, steeper but consistent with the SLACS lenses.</p><p class=\"chapter-para\">The SLACS and SL2S lenses also exhibit the trend that the slope of the density profile inferred from lensing is negatively correlated with effective radius and positively correlated with the average surface mass density contained within | ${R_{\\rm e}}/2$ |. In Fig.\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-fig4\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"fig4\" data-open=\"fig4\" class=\"link link-reveal link-table xref-fig\">4</a>, we plot these correlations for our lenses. The top panel shows the density profile slope, \u03b1, plotted against effective radius. For our four early-type lenses, we obtain a straight line fit of | $\\alpha =2.17\\pm 0.20 - (0.70\\pm 0.47)\\log ({R_{\\rm e}/1\\,{\\rm kpc}})$ |, as shown in the figure by the dotted line and grey shaded 1\u03c3 error envelope. This compares to the fit | $\\alpha =2.39\\pm 0.10 - (0.41\\pm 0.12)\\log ({R_{\\rm e}/1\\,{\\rm kpc}})$ | by Auger et\u00a0al. (<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib3\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib3\" data-open=\"bib3\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2010b</a>) for the SLACS lenses and | $\\alpha =2.05\\pm 0.06 - (0.67\\pm 0.20)\\log ({R_{\\rm e}/1\\,{\\rm kpc}})$ | by Sonnenfeld et\u00a0al. (<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib62\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib62\" data-open=\"bib62\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2013</a>) for the SL2S lenses.<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-fn4\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"fn4\" data-open=\"fn4\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-fn js-xref-fn\"><sup>3</sup></a></p>                        <a id=\"111789771\" scrollto-destination=\"111789771\"></a>\r\n<div data-id=\"fig4\" class=\"fig fig-section js-fig-section\"><div class=\"label fig-label\">Figure 4.</div><div class=\"graphic-wrap\"><img class=\"content-image\" src=\"https://oup.silverchair-cdn.com/oup/backfile/Content_public/Journal/mnras/440/3/10.1093_mnras_stu305/3/m_stu305fig4.jpeg?Expires=1611467636&amp;Signature=nUlHd-w3Tq~PH5r-xPCziMtzQucm02gPrEAaOvxhDwg5UCfVdmywBrLOvOSmw5HYH~h9KzHoeNxbVl7Te35PDUPvNcS7R3FudP5ohboNgxw37wcCezZD463JzaqAG-oKQTpwrSczvlO~str8b1aIN5p6ZgpWHZBpOkSqtBX6tFlPRGfyAPb~rUr6n1FI-zIoZDpaZAu7WJhZiyvsrtFXHovQJ~P~FoV0TFE8QGdLvim5-jroHOunQxgyBOvToo~DyfdkRnSa36N4VmztlT6BsJiIHfIeIbM9Wc9vHNevhjPqva8P6IwXzeDnDZBeI87BFwpUi5tMiJ1fwVe3Gl4gFA__&amp;Key-Pair-Id=APKAIE5G5CRDK6RD3PGA\" alt=\"Correlation between total mass density profile slope, \u03b1, and effective radius, Re, (top panel) and average projected total surface mass density within Re/2 (bottom panel). In both panels, the Sa lens J091305.0\u2212005343, coloured with a grey data point, has been omitted in the straight line fit which is shown by the dotted line and grey shaded 1\u03c3 error envelope.\" data-path-from-xml=\"stu305fig4.jpeg\" /><div class=\"fig-orig original-slide\"><a class=\"fig-view-orig js-view-large openInAnotherWindow\" href=\"/view-large/figure/111789771/stu305fig4.jpeg\" data-path-from-xml=\"stu305fig4.jpeg\" target=\"_blank\">Open in new tab</a><a data-section=\"111789771\" href=\"/DownloadFile/DownloadImage.aspx?image=https://oup.silverchair-cdn.com/oup/backfile/Content_public/Journal/mnras/440/3/10.1093_mnras_stu305/3/stu305fig4.jpeg?Expires=1611467636&Signature=HIE64J9QaEBCSkJPZU1B9~Xjgw-tipaGc-EpMAgyV0tdFY3pCMjuD-MiV9bATqHtH20UP21B2HSOgrrlno5kyWwmf50D90TNh6a3FoWY2kPKYJMozFGmLm9M~u8~u4ztmmyJJSuYoC14PooJdGwlVEKnkJCCjGcWvh8i5UHvW61b7BTNqx9QcqXxMqcequOgh2wJ87B5B5~tyMKZXKc2y-PNdcTqj41oAlKGjhwIYWIAg7XmkvZzz1524XGF9q-ZaAHY1Ah9qWSsgr2YuYerP9v8wtTdCLUbfeG7dOFDSUTibHC1UIKEvUllSzgJqWPik~72r1A~qt4bUC71hm5X2Q__&Key-Pair-Id=APKAIE5G5CRDK6RD3PGA&sec=111789771&ar=1074641&xsltPath=~/UI/app/XSLT&imagename=&siteId=5326\" class=\"download-slide\" data-path-from-xml=\"stu305fig4.jpeg\">Download slide</a></div></div><div class=\"caption fig-caption\"><p class=\"chapter-para\">Correlation between total mass density profile slope, \u03b1, and effective radius, <em>R</em><sub>e</sub>, (<em>top panel</em>) and average projected total surface mass density within <em>R</em><sub>e</sub>/2 (<em>bottom panel</em>). In both panels, the Sa lens J091305.0\u2212005343, coloured with a grey data point, has been omitted in the straight line fit which is shown by the dotted line and grey shaded 1\u03c3 error envelope.</p></div></div><div content-id=\"fig4\" class=\"fig fig-modal reveal-modal\"><div class=\"label fig-label\">Figure 4.</div><div class=\"graphic-wrap\"><img class=\"content-image\" src=\"https://oup.silverchair-cdn.com/oup/backfile/Content_public/Journal/mnras/440/3/10.1093_mnras_stu305/3/m_stu305fig4.jpeg?Expires=1611467636&amp;Signature=nUlHd-w3Tq~PH5r-xPCziMtzQucm02gPrEAaOvxhDwg5UCfVdmywBrLOvOSmw5HYH~h9KzHoeNxbVl7Te35PDUPvNcS7R3FudP5ohboNgxw37wcCezZD463JzaqAG-oKQTpwrSczvlO~str8b1aIN5p6ZgpWHZBpOkSqtBX6tFlPRGfyAPb~rUr6n1FI-zIoZDpaZAu7WJhZiyvsrtFXHovQJ~P~FoV0TFE8QGdLvim5-jroHOunQxgyBOvToo~DyfdkRnSa36N4VmztlT6BsJiIHfIeIbM9Wc9vHNevhjPqva8P6IwXzeDnDZBeI87BFwpUi5tMiJ1fwVe3Gl4gFA__&amp;Key-Pair-Id=APKAIE5G5CRDK6RD3PGA\" alt=\"Correlation between total mass density profile slope, \u03b1, and effective radius, Re, (top panel) and average projected total surface mass density within Re/2 (bottom panel). In both panels, the Sa lens J091305.0\u2212005343, coloured with a grey data point, has been omitted in the straight line fit which is shown by the dotted line and grey shaded 1\u03c3 error envelope.\" data-path-from-xml=\"stu305fig4.jpeg\" /><div class=\"fig-orig original-slide\"><a class=\"fig-view-orig js-view-large openInAnotherWindow\" href=\"/view-large/figure/111789771/stu305fig4.jpeg\" data-path-from-xml=\"stu305fig4.jpeg\" target=\"_blank\">Open in new tab</a><a data-section=\"111789771\" href=\"/DownloadFile/DownloadImage.aspx?image=https://oup.silverchair-cdn.com/oup/backfile/Content_public/Journal/mnras/440/3/10.1093_mnras_stu305/3/stu305fig4.jpeg?Expires=1611467636&Signature=HIE64J9QaEBCSkJPZU1B9~Xjgw-tipaGc-EpMAgyV0tdFY3pCMjuD-MiV9bATqHtH20UP21B2HSOgrrlno5kyWwmf50D90TNh6a3FoWY2kPKYJMozFGmLm9M~u8~u4ztmmyJJSuYoC14PooJdGwlVEKnkJCCjGcWvh8i5UHvW61b7BTNqx9QcqXxMqcequOgh2wJ87B5B5~tyMKZXKc2y-PNdcTqj41oAlKGjhwIYWIAg7XmkvZzz1524XGF9q-ZaAHY1Ah9qWSsgr2YuYerP9v8wtTdCLUbfeG7dOFDSUTibHC1UIKEvUllSzgJqWPik~72r1A~qt4bUC71hm5X2Q__&Key-Pair-Id=APKAIE5G5CRDK6RD3PGA&sec=111789771&ar=1074641&xsltPath=~/UI/app/XSLT&imagename=&siteId=5326\" class=\"download-slide\" data-path-from-xml=\"stu305fig4.jpeg\">Download slide</a></div></div><div class=\"caption fig-caption\"><p class=\"chapter-para\">Correlation between total mass density profile slope, \u03b1, and effective radius, <em>R</em><sub>e</sub>, (<em>top panel</em>) and average projected total surface mass density within <em>R</em><sub>e</sub>/2 (<em>bottom panel</em>). In both panels, the Sa lens J091305.0\u2212005343, coloured with a grey data point, has been omitted in the straight line fit which is shown by the dotted line and grey shaded 1\u03c3 error envelope.</p></div></div><p class=\"chapter-para\">In the bottom panel of Fig.\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-fig4\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"fig4\" data-open=\"fig4\" class=\"link link-reveal link-table xref-fig\">4</a>, we plot \u03b1 against the average total surface mass density within half the effective radius, as quantified by the ratio | $M{{\\rm ^{Tot}}_{R_{\\rm e}/2}/({R_{\\rm e}/1\\,{\\rm kpc}})^2}$ |. We obtain a straight line fit of | $\\alpha =11.60\\pm 0.11+ (0.35\\pm 0.22)\\log ({M^{{\\rm Tot}}_{R_{\\rm e}/2}/(R_{\\rm e}/1\\,{\\rm kpc})^2})$ | which compares to the gradient of | ${{\\rm d} \\alpha / {\\rm d} \\log [M^{{\\rm Tot}}_{R_{\\rm e}/2}/(R_{\\rm e}/1\\,{\\rm kpc})^2]=0.85\\pm 0.19}$ | for the SLACS lenses as measured by Auger et\u00a0al. (<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib3\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib3\" data-open=\"bib3\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2010b</a>).</p>                    <h2 scrollto-destination=111789773 id=\"111789773\" class=\"section-title\" data-legacy-id=sec5>5 DISCUSSION</h2>\r\n<p class=\"chapter-para\">As the preceding section has shown, in all observational diagnostics and trends we have considered, bar the correlation between <em>f</em><sub>DM</sub> and | ${R_{\\rm e}}$ |, the H-ATLAS lenses are more similar to the SL2S lenses than those of SLACS. This is perhaps not surprising when one considers the following characteristic median values expressed in order SLACS, SL2S, H-ATLAS: | $\\widetilde{R}_{\\rm e} \\simeq$ |\u00a08, 5, 3\u00a0kpc; | $\\widetilde{M}_* \\simeq 10^{11.6}$ |, 10<sup>11.5</sup>, 10<sup>11.2</sup>\u8201M<sub>\u2299</sub>; | $\\widetilde{M}^{\\rm Tot}_{R_{\\rm e}/2} \\simeq 10^{11.2}$ |, 10<sup>11.0</sup>, 10<sup>10.8</sup>\u8201M<sub>\u2299</sub>. It appears to be the case therefore, at least in the SDP data, that the H-ATLAS lenses populate the low-mass tail of the SL2S lens sample.</p><p class=\"chapter-para\">Despite these obvious differences and despite our very small sample of lenses at present, we still detect many of the correlations found in the various other aforementioned studies. These studies have taken care to ensure that the trends they detect are not the result of selection biases or systematic effects. In a similar vein, a possible systematic effect to be considered when comparing our results with these is that our lensing analysis does not incorporate any additional constraints from dynamical measurements. This means that the slope is measured in the vicinity of the Einstein ring, whereas in analyses using lensing and dynamics, the average slope interior to the Einstein ring is measured. Therefore, a change in slope with radius could potentially introduce a systematic offset in the slopes determined in the present work with respect to those from lensing and dynamics. However, as previously mentioned, on the scales probed by strong lensing, the slope appears not to exhibit any significant dependence on radius since there is no apparent trend in slope with the ratio of Einstein radius to effective radius (Koopmans et\u00a0al. <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib39\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib39\" data-open=\"bib39\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2006</a>; Ruff et\u00a0al. <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib57\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib57\" data-open=\"bib57\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2011</a>).</p><p class=\"chapter-para\">This is related to the effect reported in Ruff et\u00a0al. (<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib57\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib57\" data-open=\"bib57\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2011</a>) and Bolton et\u00a0al. (<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib9\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib9\" data-open=\"bib9\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2012</a>) that the ratio of the Einstein radius to the lens galaxy's effective radius increases with increasing lens redshift. This is due to the redshift dependence of the angular diameter distance ratios which govern the lensing geometry and the fact that a fixed physical size reduces in angular extent with increasing redshift (at least out to the lens redshifts in this work). This has the result that as redshift is increased, the density profile is measured by our lensing-only analysis at a radius which is an increasing multiple of the effective radius. A change in slope with radius would therefore mimic a change in slope with redshift. However, in addition to the observational evidence that the slope is not seen to depend on radius on strong lensing scales, on much larger scales, the slope is expected to steepen with increasing radius according to simulations and the requirement that the total halo mass converges. Therefore, even if this steepening were to influence our slope measurements, our detection of the rate at which slopes become less steep with increasing redshift must be a lower limit to the intrinsic rate.</p><p class=\"chapter-para\">In terms of a physical interpretation of observed variations in the density profile slope, the picture is somewhat unclear. Simulations by Dubois et\u00a0al. (<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib23\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib23\" data-open=\"bib23\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2013</a>) reproduce the observed steepening with decreasing redshift and find that feedback from active galactic nuclei (AGN) modifies the slope. This work indicates that AGN feedback is required to reproduce the near-isothermal profiles (i.e. \u03b1 \u2243 2) observed in low-redshift early-type galaxies. However, the simulations of Remus et\u00a0al. (<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib56\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib56\" data-open=\"bib56\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2013</a>) indicate that whilst a combination of dry minor and major mergers produce near-isothermality at low redshifts, the slopes are significantly steeper at higher redshift. Confounding this is the simulation work of Nipoti, Treu &amp; Bolton (<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib50\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib50\" data-open=\"bib50\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2009</a>) which shows that the total mass profile of early types is not modified at all by dry mergers.</p><p class=\"chapter-para\">Turning to the projected dark matter fraction within half the effective radius, <em>f</em><sub>DM</sub>, Dubois et\u00a0al. (<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib23\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib23\" data-open=\"bib23\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2013</a>) claim that AGN feedback is required to reproduce the observed fractions and that without it, the fraction of stellar mass is too high. The SLACS work reports a 5\u03c3 detection of increasing <em>f</em><sub>DM</sub> with effective radius. This compares to our marginal detection (2.3\u03c3) and a null detection in the SL2S lenses. If this trend is real, an obvious interpretation might be that star formation efficiency reduces as halo mass increases. Another possibility is presented by Nipoti et\u00a0al. (<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib50\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib50\" data-open=\"bib50\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2009</a>) who predict that the fraction of dark matter within the effective radius increases as a result of mergers.</p><p class=\"chapter-para\">Instead of investigating how <em>f</em><sub>DM</sub> varies with effective radius, an alternative is to test whether <em>f</em><sub>DM</sub> changes with redshift since this is another diagnostic which can be provided by simulations.</p><p class=\"chapter-para\">Fig.\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-fig5\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"fig5\" data-open=\"fig5\" class=\"link link-reveal link-table xref-fig\">5</a> shows this plot for the H-ATLAS lenses (excluding J091305.0\u2212005343). We measure a straight line fit of <em>f</em><sub>DM</sub>\u00a0=\u00a00.23\u00a0\u00b1 0.09 + (0.32 \u00b1 0.14)| $z$ |. In comparison, the simulations of Dubois et\u00a0al. (<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib23\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib23\" data-open=\"bib23\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2013</a>) predict that the fraction of dark matter within 10\u00a0per\u8201cent of the virial radius decreases with increasing redshift when AGN feedback is present, or, that this fraction remains constant with redshift if AGN feedback is not present. If the fraction of dark matter within 10\u00a0per\u8201cent of the virial radius scales in the same way as <em>f</em><sub>DM</sub>, then this is in contrast to our findings. However, since <em>f</em><sub>DM</sub> depends on the size of the stellar component and the virial radius effectively does not, there is still the possibility that the two results are consistent if the stellar mass increases in spatial extent relative to the dark matter with increasing redshift.</p>                        <a id=\"111789781\" scrollto-destination=\"111789781\"></a>\r\n<div data-id=\"fig5\" class=\"fig fig-section js-fig-section\"><div class=\"label fig-label\">Figure 5.</div><div class=\"graphic-wrap\"><img class=\"content-image\" src=\"https://oup.silverchair-cdn.com/oup/backfile/Content_public/Journal/mnras/440/3/10.1093_mnras_stu305/3/m_stu305fig5.jpeg?Expires=1611467636&amp;Signature=bE44ckpIiptIzXzPbQxMk~jv6tRCl6GNTssjVmJ1ZYCe-3SjXu3Y3SsNN8JsQ4iy9h9SoU1UwU-FroBqDwMYi8EzvoXkDvWC3-YtuSrW7qIhg6tHKnSA4EoMJ2~yw3PQOopQeFslQZU-ltR3Q6297aa1QkYmNXBM-e5ryIieTkbavSIS2hYwdPESqJejlZj9fnYz80vSRjv4cCM8qEWc8QaIhy1nQhFQwiabewrC~2cNMz5KC17frChdNFBaB6Oa-HjCHhETNuI1fXFml4KeEjH13idy6TpwpyPcAh6EDIuu5Nlhmmah4rJkQvhR~DHhztuWj~eytsrhYWdj07v-kg__&amp;Key-Pair-Id=APKAIE5G5CRDK6RD3PGA\" alt=\"Variation of the fraction of dark matter within half the effective radius, fDM, for a Salpeter IMF with redshift. The grey shading depicts the 1\u03c3 error region for the straight line fit.\" data-path-from-xml=\"stu305fig5.jpeg\" /><div class=\"fig-orig original-slide\"><a class=\"fig-view-orig js-view-large openInAnotherWindow\" href=\"/view-large/figure/111789781/stu305fig5.jpeg\" data-path-from-xml=\"stu305fig5.jpeg\" target=\"_blank\">Open in new tab</a><a data-section=\"111789781\" href=\"/DownloadFile/DownloadImage.aspx?image=https://oup.silverchair-cdn.com/oup/backfile/Content_public/Journal/mnras/440/3/10.1093_mnras_stu305/3/stu305fig5.jpeg?Expires=1611467636&Signature=vXfodpP8pG0RDn2oKI9TNKsLnL4qCqNnqwrWwgok9sH2Q9lSrR9p4dRabH1KG~6D0OgSaFMA8Prm3lb~W3xp13jN9Ga4EoGkDgLmJgae5PHcatAC5Y~PtZzeeCBC3BZPJ6Nqld7kZlN2YYf~zwJLhhEfk2TlWKMf4SwMWgzz3UYLqtVJxaxss88yG2svvqtkujo~LtOHnrj8E8~Ls-3trapqQTmdWa8cUECVlU~EYyv~uyOb5KAC0hB13ix2xXmzHi0XW9zQ0odYVnSMYHWUUg-surv23LkCq7tIEftyjj8z2hxsDti-Q0GOBPNY6l1ETKHM8fN~ORVpdfyodBPuKQ__&Key-Pair-Id=APKAIE5G5CRDK6RD3PGA&sec=111789781&ar=1074641&xsltPath=~/UI/app/XSLT&imagename=&siteId=5326\" class=\"download-slide\" data-path-from-xml=\"stu305fig5.jpeg\">Download slide</a></div></div><div class=\"caption fig-caption\"><p class=\"chapter-para\">Variation of the fraction of dark matter within half the effective radius, <em>f</em><sub>DM</sub>, for a Salpeter IMF with redshift. The grey shading depicts the 1\u03c3 error region for the straight line fit.</p></div></div><div content-id=\"fig5\" class=\"fig fig-modal reveal-modal\"><div class=\"label fig-label\">Figure 5.</div><div class=\"graphic-wrap\"><img class=\"content-image\" src=\"https://oup.silverchair-cdn.com/oup/backfile/Content_public/Journal/mnras/440/3/10.1093_mnras_stu305/3/m_stu305fig5.jpeg?Expires=1611467636&amp;Signature=bE44ckpIiptIzXzPbQxMk~jv6tRCl6GNTssjVmJ1ZYCe-3SjXu3Y3SsNN8JsQ4iy9h9SoU1UwU-FroBqDwMYi8EzvoXkDvWC3-YtuSrW7qIhg6tHKnSA4EoMJ2~yw3PQOopQeFslQZU-ltR3Q6297aa1QkYmNXBM-e5ryIieTkbavSIS2hYwdPESqJejlZj9fnYz80vSRjv4cCM8qEWc8QaIhy1nQhFQwiabewrC~2cNMz5KC17frChdNFBaB6Oa-HjCHhETNuI1fXFml4KeEjH13idy6TpwpyPcAh6EDIuu5Nlhmmah4rJkQvhR~DHhztuWj~eytsrhYWdj07v-kg__&amp;Key-Pair-Id=APKAIE5G5CRDK6RD3PGA\" alt=\"Variation of the fraction of dark matter within half the effective radius, fDM, for a Salpeter IMF with redshift. The grey shading depicts the 1\u03c3 error region for the straight line fit.\" data-path-from-xml=\"stu305fig5.jpeg\" /><div class=\"fig-orig original-slide\"><a class=\"fig-view-orig js-view-large openInAnotherWindow\" href=\"/view-large/figure/111789781/stu305fig5.jpeg\" data-path-from-xml=\"stu305fig5.jpeg\" target=\"_blank\">Open in new tab</a><a data-section=\"111789781\" href=\"/DownloadFile/DownloadImage.aspx?image=https://oup.silverchair-cdn.com/oup/backfile/Content_public/Journal/mnras/440/3/10.1093_mnras_stu305/3/stu305fig5.jpeg?Expires=1611467636&Signature=vXfodpP8pG0RDn2oKI9TNKsLnL4qCqNnqwrWwgok9sH2Q9lSrR9p4dRabH1KG~6D0OgSaFMA8Prm3lb~W3xp13jN9Ga4EoGkDgLmJgae5PHcatAC5Y~PtZzeeCBC3BZPJ6Nqld7kZlN2YYf~zwJLhhEfk2TlWKMf4SwMWgzz3UYLqtVJxaxss88yG2svvqtkujo~LtOHnrj8E8~Ls-3trapqQTmdWa8cUECVlU~EYyv~uyOb5KAC0hB13ix2xXmzHi0XW9zQ0odYVnSMYHWUUg-surv23LkCq7tIEftyjj8z2hxsDti-Q0GOBPNY6l1ETKHM8fN~ORVpdfyodBPuKQ__&Key-Pair-Id=APKAIE5G5CRDK6RD3PGA&sec=111789781&ar=1074641&xsltPath=~/UI/app/XSLT&imagename=&siteId=5326\" class=\"download-slide\" data-path-from-xml=\"stu305fig5.jpeg\">Download slide</a></div></div><div class=\"caption fig-caption\"><p class=\"chapter-para\">Variation of the fraction of dark matter within half the effective radius, <em>f</em><sub>DM</sub>, for a Salpeter IMF with redshift. The grey shading depicts the 1\u03c3 error region for the straight line fit.</p></div></div><p class=\"chapter-para\">Important clues also come from comparing the morphology of the visible component of the lenses with that of the dark matter halo. We find significant discrepancies in the alignment and ellipticity between the stellar component and the total mass in some lenses. The discrepancies are consistent with what has been measured in the SL2S sample but larger than those found in SLACS. This may be a combination of the fact that both the H-ATLAS and SL2S lenses have a higher average ratio of Einstein radius to effective radius than the SLACS lenses and that the baryonic morphology correlates less strongly with that of the dark matter at larger radii (e.g. Bett et\u00a0al. <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib6\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib6\" data-open=\"bib6\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2010</a>; Skibba &amp; Macci\u00f2 <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib61\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib61\" data-open=\"bib61\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2011</a>). In order to proceed with a more robust interpretation of these findings, more input is required from simulation work although as we previously discussed, present indications are that such large morphological differences between the dark and baryonic components are to be expected.</p>                    <h2 scrollto-destination=111789783 id=\"111789783\" class=\"section-title\" data-legacy-id=sec6>6 SUMMARY</h2>\r\n<p class=\"chapter-para\">In this paper, we have modelled the first five strong gravitational lens systems discovered in the H-ATLAS SDP data. To directly compare with other lensing studies, we have modelled the lenses with elliptical power-law density profiles and searched for trends in the power-law slope and the fraction of dark matter contained within half the effective radius. We have found consistency with almost all existing lens analyses, although with our present sample of only five lenses, we lack high statistical significance in our measured trends. The main results of this paper are that: <ul class=\"roman-lower\"><li><p class=\"chapter-para\">the slope of the power-law density profile varies with redshift according to \u03b1\u00a0=\u00a02.05 \u00b1 0.08\u00a0\u2212\u00a0(0.30 \u00b1 0.14)| $z$ |.</p></li><li><p class=\"chapter-para\">the H-ATLAS lenses have a mean projected dark matter fraction within half the effective radius of <em>f</em><sub>DM</sub>\u00a0=\u00a00.46 with a scatter of 0.10, for a Salpeter IMF;</p></li><li><p class=\"chapter-para\">the dark matter fraction within half the effective radius scales with effective radius as | $f_{\\rm DM}=-0.01\\pm 0.20 + (1.04\\pm 0.46)\\log ({R_{\\rm e}/1\\,{\\rm kpc}})$ |;</p></li><li><p class=\"chapter-para\">the slope of the power-law density profile scales with effective radius as | $\\alpha =2.17\\pm 0.20 - (0.70\\pm 0.47)\\log ({R_{\\rm e} / 1\\,{\\rm kpc}})$ | and with the average total surface mass density within half the effective radius, as quantified by the ratio | $M{^{{\\rm Tot}}_{R_{\\rm e}/2}/(R_{\\rm e}/1\\,{\\rm kpc})^2}$ | as | $M{^{{\\rm Tot}}_{R_{\\rm e}/2}/(R_{\\rm e}/1\\,{\\rm kpc})^2}$ |;</p></li><li><p class=\"chapter-para\"><em>f</em><sub>DM</sub> scales with redshift as <em>f</em><sub>DM</sub>\u00a0=\u00a00.23 \u00b1 0.09 + (0.32 \u00b1 0.14)| $z$ |.</p></li></ul></p><p class=\"chapter-para\">The modelling in this paper used near-IR <em>HST</em> data. Whilst the <em>HST</em> provides the high-resolution imaging necessary for modelling of high-redshift lenses, not all of the H-ATLAS lensed sources will be as readily detected in the near-IR as the SDP lenses considered herein. Being submm selected systems, submm and radio interferometry is the ideal technology for obtaining the required signal to noise and image resolution. This has been demonstrated by (<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib14\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib14\" data-open=\"bib14\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">B13</a>) who have used the SMA to image several tens of lenses detected by the <em>Herschel Space Observatory</em>. Atacama Large Millimetre Array (ALMA) has also been used to image some of the SPT lenses (see, for example Hezaveh et\u00a0al. <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib33\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib33\" data-open=\"bib33\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2013</a>). However, the true power of this facility will not be realized until it operates with its full complement of antennas. At this point, ALMA will begin to deliver the high signal-to-noise and high-resolution images required by source-inversion lens modelling methods, necessary for the strongest possible constraints on galaxy mass profiles. Furthermore, spectral line imaging with ALMA will open up the possibility of reconstructing lensed source velocity maps to probe the dynamics of high-redshift submm galaxies.</p><p class=\"chapter-para\">The H-ATLAS lens sample is very much in its infancy. As the size of the sample grows and begins to populate the | $z$ |<sub>d</sub> \u2243 1 realm and beyond, constraints on the evolution of mass in galaxies will continue to strengthen.</p><p class=\"chapter-para\">The work in this paper is based on observations made with the NASA/ESA <em>Hubble Space Telescope</em> under the <em>HST</em> programme #12194. MN acknowledges financial support from ASI/INAF Agreement I/072/09/0 and from PRIN-INAF 2012 project: \u2018Looking into the dust-obscured phase of galaxy formation through cosmic zoom lenses in the Herschel Astrophysical Large Area Survey\u2019. JGN acknowledges financial support from the Spanish Ministerio de Ciencia e Innovacion, project AYA2010-21766-C03-01, and the Spanish CSIC for a JAE-DOC fellowship, co-funded by the European Social Fund. We thank Martin Baes and Michal Michalowski for constructive comments on this paper. We would also like to thank Adam Moss for technical discussions regarding the MCMC analysis contained herein.</p><div content-id=\"fn1\"><span class=\"fn\"><span class=\"label fn-label\"><span rel=\"nofollow\" data-fn-id=\"fn1\" class=\"end-note-link\">\u2605</span></span><div class=\"footnote-content\"><p class=\"footnote-compatibility\"><em>Herschel</em> is an ESA space observatory with science instruments provided by European-led Principal Investigator consortia and with important participation from NASA.</p></div></span></div><div content-id=\"fn2\"><span class=\"fn\"><span class=\"label fn-label\"><span rel=\"nofollow\" data-fn-id=\"fn2\" class=\"end-note-link\">1</span></span><div class=\"footnote-content\"><p class=\"footnote-compatibility\">In practice, we have opted to set the same regularization weight across all images to simplify the MCMC minimization.</p></div></span></div><div content-id=\"fn3\"><span class=\"fn\"><span class=\"label fn-label\"><span rel=\"nofollow\" data-fn-id=\"fn3\" class=\"end-note-link\">2</span></span><div class=\"footnote-content\"><p class=\"footnote-compatibility\">We note that the lens elongations derived by <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib14\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib14\" data-open=\"bib14\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">B13</a> are consistently higher than those derived in our study and we attribute this, at least partly, to the lack of external shear in the <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib14\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib14\" data-open=\"bib14\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">B13</a> lens model.</p></div></span></div><div content-id=\"fn4\"><span class=\"fn\"><span class=\"label four-dimensional plane that Sonnenfeld et\u00a0al. fit to the density profile slope by assuming a redshift of 0.3 and a stellar mass of | $\\log ({M_*/{\\rm M}_{\\odot }})=11.5$ |.</p></div></span></div>                    <h2 scrollto-destination=111789794 id=\"111789794\" class=\"backreferences-title\" >REFERENCES</h2>\r\n<div class=\"ref-list\"><div content-id=\"bib1a\" href=\"https://s100.copyright.com/AppDispatchServlet?publisherName=OUP&amp;publication=1365-2966&amp;title=%26lt%3Bem%26gt%3BHerschel%26lt%3B%2Fem%26gt%3B%26lt%3Bspan%20class%3D%26quot%3BxrefLink%26quot%3B%20id%3D%26quot%3Bjumplink-fn1%26quot%3B%26gt%3B%26lt%3B%2Fspan%26gt%3B%26lt%3Ba%20href%3D%26quot%3Bjavascript%3A%3B%26quot%3B%20reveal-id%3D%26quot%3Bfn1%26quot%3B%20data-open%3D%26quot%3Bfn1%26quot%3B%20class%3D%26quot%3Blink%20link-ref%20link-reveal%20xref-fn%20js-xref-fn%26quot%3B%26gt%3B%26lt%3Bsup%26gt%3B%E2%98%85%26lt%3B%2Fsup%26gt%3B%26lt%3B%2Fa%26gt%3B-ATLAS%3A%20modelling%20the%20first%20strong%20gravitational%20lenses&amp;publicationDate=2014-04-04&amp;volumeNum=440&amp;issueNum=3&amp;author=Dye%2C%20S.%3B%20Negrello%2C%20M.&amp;startPage=2013&amp;endPage=2025&amp;contentId=10.1093%2Fmnras%2Fstu305&amp;oa=&amp;copyright=Oxford%20University%20Press&amp;orderBeanReset=True\" class=\"center-inner-row\">\r\n        <nav class=\"navbar-menu\">\r\n\r\n        </nav>\r\n            <div class=\"navbar-search-container js-navbar-search-container\">\r\n                <a href=\"javascript:;\" class=\"navbar-search-close js_close-navsearch\">Close</a>\r\n                    <div class=\"navbar-search\">\r\n                        <div id=\"MicrositeSearch\" class=\"icon-menu_search js_expand-navsearch\"><span class=\"screenreader-text\">Search Menu</span></a></div>\r\n    </div>\r\n</div>\r\n\r\n<input id=\"hfEnableOupOnlineProductsFeatures\" name=\"hfEnableOupOnlineProductsFeatures\" type=\"hidden\" value=\"False\" />\r\n\r\n<input id=\"routename\" name=\"RouteName\" type=\"hidden\" value=\"mnras\" /> \n    </div>\n\r\n                    </div>\r\n                </div>\r\n            </div>\r\n            <div id=\"ContentTab\" class=\"content active\">\r\n    <div class=\"widget widget-ArticleFulltext widget-instance-OUP_Article_FullText_Widget\">\n        <div class=\"module-widget\">\r\n    <div class=\"widget-items\" data-widgetname=\"ArticleFulltext\">\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n<section class=\"abstract\"><p class=\"chapter-para\">We have determined the mass density radial profiles of the first five strong gravitational lens systems discovered by the <em>Herschel</em> Astrophysical Terahertz Large Area Survey. We present an enhancement of the semilinear lens inversion method of Warren &amp; Dye which allows simultaneous reconstruction of several different wavebands and apply this to dual-band imaging of the lenses acquired with the <em>Hubble Space Telescope</em>. The five systems analysed here have lens redshifts which span a range 0.22 \u2264 | $z$ | \u2264 0.94. Our findings are consistent with other studies by concluding that: (1) the logarithmic slope of the total mass density profile steepens with decreasing redshift; (2) the slope is positively correlated with the average total projected mass density of the lens contained within half the effective radius and negatively correlated with the effective radius; (3) the fraction of dark matter contained within half the effective radius increases with increasing effective radius and increases with redshift.</p></section>                        <div class=\"article-metadata-panel clearfix\"></div>\r\n<div class=\"kwd-group\"><a class=\"kwd-part kwd-main\" href=\"javascript:;\" data-keyword=\"&quot;galaxies: evolution&quot;\">galaxies: evolution</a>, <a class=\"kwd-part kwd-main\" href=\"javascript:;\" data-keyword=\"&quot;galaxies: structure&quot;\">galaxies: structure</a></div>                    <h2 scrollto-destination=111789702 id=\"111789702\" class=\"section-title\" data-legacy-id=sec1>1 INTRODUCTION</h2>\r\n<p class=\"chapter-para\">Early-type galaxies, despite being relatively well studied, continue to challenge our complete understanding of their formation and evolution. Current unanswered questions include quantifying the role of mergers in their evolution (e.g. van Dokkum et\u00a0al. <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib74\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib74\" data-open=\"bib74\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">1999</a>; Khochfar &amp; Burkert <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib38\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib38\" data-open=\"bib38\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2003</a>; Bell et\u00a0al. <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib5\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib5\" data-open=\"bib5\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2006</a>; Hilz, Naab &amp; Ostriker <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib34\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib34\" data-open=\"bib34\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2013</a>), reliably determining their stellar build-up and reconciling this with downsizing (e.g. Thomas et\u00a0al. <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib66\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib66\" data-open=\"bib66\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2005</a>; Maraston et\u00a0al. <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib46\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib46\" data-open=\"bib46\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2009</a>; Tojeiro et\u00a0al. <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib67\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib67\" data-open=\"bib67\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2012</a>), understanding the evolution of the upper end of the mass function (e.g. Bundy, Ellis &amp; Conselice <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib12\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib12\" data-open=\"bib12\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2005</a>; Bundy, Treu &amp; Ellis <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib13\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib13\" data-open=\"bib13\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2007</a>; van der Wel et\u00a0al. <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib73\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib73\" data-open=\"bib73\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2009</a>; Hopkins et\u00a0al. <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib35\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib35\" data-open=\"bib35\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2010</a>) and identifying the process(es) by which the high-redshift population becomes dramatically less compact at low redshifts (e.g. Daddi et\u00a0al. <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib21\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib21\" data-open=\"bib21\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2005</a>; Trujillo et\u00a0al. <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib72\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib72\" data-open=\"bib72\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2006</a>; van Dokkum et\u00a0al. <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib75\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib75\" data-open=\"bib75\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2008</a>; Lani et\u00a0al. <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib41\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib41\" data-open=\"bib41\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2013</a>). Regarding this last point, mergers have been suggested as the cause of the effect, but there is much disagreement (e.g. Hopkins et\u00a0al. <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib35\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib35\" data-open=\"bib35\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2010</a>; Oser et\u00a0al. <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib52\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib52\" data-open=\"bib52\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2012</a>). Notwithstanding these unknowns, it seems likely that common formation and evolution mechanisms are at play, given the tightness of observed relationships such as the Fundamental Plane, the correlation of black hole mass with central velocity dispersion and the near-isothermality of total mass density profiles.</p><p class=\"chapter-para\">Regarding the measurement of density profiles, this has recently become a very active pursuit within the field, motivated by the many scientific applications made possible. These applications include the provision of an observational benchmark for simulations of large-scale structure formation, constraining the initial mass function (IMF) by comparing with stellar synthesis masses (e.g. Auger et\u00a0al. <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib2\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib2\" data-open=\"bib2\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2010a</a>; Treu et\u00a0al. <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib70\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib70\" data-open=\"bib70\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2010</a>; Barnab\u00e8 et\u00a0al. <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib4\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib4\" data-open=\"bib4\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2013</a>), determining the Hubble constant from gravitational lens time delays (e.g. Tewes et\u00a0al. <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib65\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib65\" data-open=\"bib65\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2013</a>, see also the review by Jackson <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib36\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib36\" data-open=\"bib36\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2007</a>) and cosmography (see Treu <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib68\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib68\" data-open=\"bib68\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2010b</a>, and references therein). Other novel applications include using density profiles of strongly lensed systems embedded within a cluster or group environment as a direct probe of the larger scale gravitational potential (e.g. Dye et\u00a0al. <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib27\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib27\" data-open=\"bib27\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2007</a>; Limousin et\u00a0al. <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib42\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib42\" data-open=\"bib42\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2010</a>) and making predictions of the self-annihilation signal of dark matter to guide annihilation detection experiments (e.g. Walker et\u00a0al. <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib78\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib78\" data-open=\"bib78\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2011</a>).</p><p class=\"chapter-para\">A debate that continues to be rekindled is the issue of whether density profiles are cored, whereby the density tends to a constant value towards small radii or whether they are cuspy, whereby the density continues to increase as a radial power law. To the advocates of cuspy profiles, the debate is over the extent to which they are cuspy, i.e. the exponent of the radial power law. The motivation that drives these studies originates from comparing observed density profiles with those predicted by <em>N</em>-body simulations of large-scale structure formation.</p><p class=\"chapter-para\">Early simulations favoured cuspy profiles that are typically steeper than those inferred from observations (see, for example, de Blok &amp; Bosma <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib22\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib22\" data-open=\"bib22\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2002</a>, and citations to this work). However, these early studies were largely based on pure dark matter simulations which ignored the effects of baryons. Accordingly, the observational data concentrated on dwarf galaxies where baryons behave more like test particles in a dominating dark matter potential. Recent work by Cole et\u00a0al. (<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib19\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib19\" data-open=\"bib19\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2012</a>) shows that cuspy dark matter profiles in simulated dwarf spheroidals results in stronger dynamical friction causing globular clusters to fall into the centres on a dynamical time-scale, in contrast to what is observed in these systems.</p><p class=\"chapter-para\">Simulations of large-scale structure are now beginning to incorporate baryons, but this is a highly complex task, fraught with many complicating factors such as black hole accretion and their subsequent feedback (e.g. Croton <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib20\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib20\" data-open=\"bib20\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2006</a>; Ciotti, Ostriker &amp; Proga <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib18\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib18\" data-open=\"bib18\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2009</a>; Bryan et\u00a0al. <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib11\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib11\" data-open=\"bib11\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2013</a>), feedback from supernovae and cooling (see, for example, Duffy et\u00a0al. <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib24\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib24\" data-open=\"bib24\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2010</a>; Newton &amp; Kay <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib49\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib49\" data-open=\"bib49\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2013</a>). Understanding the interplay of baryons and dark matter is not only essential to a full comprehension of the formation and evolution of galaxies, but can also shed light on the properties of the dark matter itself, such as constraining the self-interaction cross-section of dark matter (Spergel &amp; Steinhardt <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib63\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib63\" data-open=\"bib63\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2000</a>; Loeb &amp; Weiner <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib43\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib43\" data-open=\"bib43\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2011</a>). In this regard, Lovell et\u00a0al. (<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib44\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib44\" data-open=\"bib44\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2012</a>) find that the velocity profiles of satellite galaxies around the Milky Way are considerably better matched by warm dark matter density profiles than cold dark matter density profiles.</p><p class=\"chapter-para\">Improving the quality of observations of density profiles of galaxies, particularly the more poorly understood early types, therefore provides a much needed benchmark to assist in discrimination of the many different scenarios describing their history. Gravitational lensing offers a very powerful and yet conceptually simple approach to achieving this, independent of assumptions about the kinematical state of the deflecting mass. Whilst weak galaxy\u2013galaxy lensing can be used to constrain density profiles, this, by necessity, must be conducted in a statistical sense (e.g. Velander et\u00a0al. <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib76\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib76\" data-open=\"bib76\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2014</a>) and provides measurements of the density profile on larger scales where the dark matter dominates. Conversely, strong galaxy\u2013galaxy lensing is applicable on a per-galaxy basis and is sensitive to density profiles on small scales where the poorly constrained baryon physics is more dominant.</p><p class=\"chapter-para\">Early-type galaxies have a higher average lensing cross-section than disc galaxies (e.g. Maoz &amp; Rix <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib45\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib45\" data-open=\"bib45\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">1993</a>) and hence strong galaxy\u2013galaxy lens samples tend to harbour significantly more early- than late-type lenses. In this way, such samples provide a perfect opportunity to gain unique insights into the formation of early types, a fact that has inspired the culmination of several different lens samples to date. Recent strong galaxy\u2013galaxy lens samples include the Sloan Lens ACS (SLACS) survey (Bolton et\u00a0al. <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib7\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib7\" data-open=\"bib7\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2006</a>) with 85 lenses out to a redshift of | $z$ | \u2243 0.4 (median redshift \u22430.2; Auger et\u00a0al. <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib1\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib1\" data-open=\"bib1\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2009</a>), the Strong Lensing Legacy Survey (SL2S; Cabanac et\u00a0al. <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib15\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib15\" data-open=\"bib15\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2007</a>) with a final total of 36 lenses in the range 0.2 \u2264 | $z$ | \u2264 0.8 (median redshift \u22430.5; Sonnenfeld et\u00a0al. <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib62\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib62\" data-open=\"bib62\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2013</a>), the Sloan WFC Edge-on Late-type Lens Survey (SWELLS; Treu et\u00a0al. <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib71\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib71\" data-open=\"bib71\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2011</a>) with 20 disc galaxy lenses at | $z$ | \u2272 0.2 (Dutton et\u00a0al. <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib25\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib25\" data-open=\"bib25\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2013</a>) and 20 lenses at 0.4 \u2264 | $z$ | \u2264 0.7 identified in the Baryon Oscillation Spectroscopic Survey (BOSS; Eisenstein et\u00a0al. <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib30\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib30\" data-open=\"bib30\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2011</a>) which constitute the BOSS Emission-Line Lens Survey (BELLS; Brownstein et\u00a0al. <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib10\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib10\" data-open=\"bib10\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2012</a>). Regardless of their selection as lensing galaxies, both the SWELLS and the SLACS samples are found to be statistically consistent with being drawn at random from their parent unlensed samples with the same mass and redshift distributions (Bolton et\u00a0al. <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib8\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib8\" data-open=\"bib8\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2008</a>; Treu et\u00a0al. <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib71\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib71\" data-open=\"bib71\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2011</a>). This fortifies the role of lens samples in their aforementioned applications.</p><p class=\"chapter-para\">Analysis of these existing lens surveys has already enabled some interesting insights into galaxy evolution. For example, the fraction of dark matter within half the effective radius of early types increases with galaxy size and mass (Auger et\u00a0al. <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib3\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib3\" data-open=\"bib3\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2010b</a>; Ruff et\u00a0al. <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib57\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib57\" data-open=\"bib57\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2011</a>). Another intriguing result which has direct consequences for simulations of large-scale structure is that the total density profile of early-type galaxies appears to steepen with decreasing redshift (Ruff et\u00a0al. <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib57\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib57\" data-open=\"bib57\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2011</a>; Bolton et\u00a0al. <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib9\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib9\" data-open=\"bib9\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2012</a>; Sonnenfeld et\u00a0al. <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib62\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib62\" data-open=\"bib62\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2013</a>) although the degree to which this occurs is currently in disagreement.</p><p class=\"chapter-para\">In this paper, we present modelling of the first five strong galaxy\u2013galaxy lens systems identified in the <em>Herschel</em> Astrophysical Terahertz Large Area Survey (H-ATLAS; Eales et\u00a0al. <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib29\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib29\" data-open=\"bib29\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2010</a>). The H-ATLAS is a large-area survey (\u223c550 deg<sup>2</sup>) conducted in five passbands in the submillimetre (submm) wavelength range | $100\\lesssim \\lambda \\lesssim 500\\, \\mu$ |m using the <em>Herschel Space Observatory</em> (Pilbratt et\u00a0al. <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib54\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib54\" data-open=\"bib54\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2010</a>). Being a submm survey, the negative <em>K</em>-correction afforded by submm galaxies means that lensed sources are much more readily detected out to significantly greater redshifts than surveys conducted at optical wavelengths. Increasing the distance of a source increases the probability of it being lensed by intervening matter. Combining this fact with the large areal coverage of H-ATLAS results in an anticipated sample of hundreds of strong galaxy\u2013galaxy lenses (Negrello et\u00a0al. <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib48\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib48\" data-open=\"bib48\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2010</a>; Gonz\u00e1lez-Nuevo et\u00a0al. <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib32\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib32\" data-open=\"bib32\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2012</a>).</p><p class=\"chapter-para\">Such lenses also have the advantage that their submm emission is unaffected by any dust in the lens which means that a clean view of the source is obtained. This proves particularly important when reconstructing high-resolution surface brightness maps of the high-redshift lensed source for morphological studies. Furthermore, submm galaxy number counts are steep so that strongly lensed galaxies can be straightforwardly identified with simple flux selection criteria. This simple technique has also been applied by the Herschel Multi-tiered Extragalactic Survey (HerMES; Oliver et\u00a0al. <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib51\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib51\" data-open=\"bib51\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2012</a>. See Wardlow et\u00a0al. <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib79\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib79\" data-open=\"bib79\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2013</a>, for an account of the lensing aspects of this survey) and the survey carried out at mm wavelengths by the South Pole Telescope (Carlstrom et\u00a0al. <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib16\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib16\" data-open=\"bib16\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2011</a>; Vieira et\u00a0al. <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib77\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib77\" data-open=\"bib77\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2013</a>).</p><p class=\"chapter-para\">The ultimate size of the H-ATLAS sample is an obvious advantage in terms of improving statistical uncertainties. However, another far more compelling benefit that arises from the negative <em>K</em>-correction in the submm is that higher redshift lensed sources are more likely to be lensed by higher redshift lenses. From an evolutionary point of view, this brings about an increase in the period over which transformations in density profiles can be determined, back to earlier times in the Universe's history when the rate of galaxy evolution was stronger (for a theoretical perspective, see, for e.g. Schaye et\u00a0al. <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib59\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib59\" data-open=\"bib59\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2010</a>). In addition to this, almost all submm galaxies have extended structure on the scales of typical galaxy lens caustics so that their lensed images comprise extended arcs and ring-like structures. As demonstrated by Dye &amp; Warren (<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib26\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib26\" data-open=\"bib26\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2005</a>) in application of the semilinear inversion (SLI) algorithm (Warren &amp; Dye <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib80\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib80\" data-open=\"bib80\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2003</a>), extended structure in lensed images allows stronger constraints to be placed on the density profile of the lensing galaxy.</p><p class=\"chapter-para\">In this paper, we apply an enhanced version of the SLI method which allows multiple data sets observed at different wavelengths to be simultaneously reconstructed with the same lens model. We apply this to <em>Hubble Space Telescope</em> (<em>HST</em>) images acquired in both the <em>F</em>110\u8201<em>W</em> and <em>F</em>160\u8201<em>W</em> filters of each of the five lenses identified in the 14.4\u00a0deg<sup>2</sup> data released by the H-ATLAS consortium as science demonstration phase (SDP) data.</p><p class=\"chapter-para\">The layout of this paper is as follows: Section\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-sec2\"></span><a href=\"#sec2\" class=\"sectionLink xref-sec\">2</a> outlines the data. In Section\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-sec3\"></span><a href=\"#sec3\" class=\"sectionLink xref-sec\">3</a>, we describe the methodology of the lens modelling, including a description of the enhanced SLI method. Section\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-sec4\"></span><a href=\"#sec4\" class=\"sectionLink xref-sec\">4</a> presents the results and we summarize the findings of this work in Section\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-sec6\"></span><a href=\"#sec6\" class=\"sectionLink xref-sec\">6</a>. Throughout this paper, we assume the following cosmological parameters; <em>H</em><sub>0</sub>\u00a0=\u00a067\u8201km\u8201s<sup>\u22121</sup>\u8201Mpc<sup>\u22121</sup>, | $\\Omega _{\\rm m}=0.32$ |, \u03a9<sub>\u039b</sub>\u00a0=\u00a00.68 [Ade et\u00a0al. (Planck Collaboration) <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib1a\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib1a\" data-open=\"bib1a\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2013</a>].</p>                    <h2 scrollto-destination=111789716 id=\"111789716\" class=\"section-title\" data-legacy-id=sec2>2 DATA</h2>\r\n<p class=\"chapter-para\">The data analysed in this work are more thoroughly described in a companion paper (Negrello et\u00a0al. <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib49a\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib49a\" data-open=\"bib49a\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2014</a>, hereafter denoted <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib49a\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib49a\" data-open=\"bib49a\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">N14</a>) but we include the pertinent details here for completeness.</p><p class=\"chapter-para\">The <em>HST</em> observations were carried out in 2011 April in Cycle 18 under proposal 12194 (PI Negrello) using the Wide Field Camera 3 (WFC3). Two orbits were allocated per target with at least three quarters of the total exposure time per target of 5130\u00a0s acquired in the <em>F</em>160\u8201<em>W</em> filter and the remainder in the <em>F</em>110\u8201<em>W</em> filter. Images were reduced using the <span class=\"small-caps\">iraf</span><span class=\"small-caps\">multidrizzle</span> package and resampled to a pixel scale of 0.064\u00a0arcsec, half the intrinsic pixel scale of the WFC3.</p><p class=\"chapter-para\">The lens galaxy flux and lensed background source image in each system were then simultaneously fitted with smooth profiles using the <span class=\"small-caps\">galfit</span> software (Peng et\u00a0al. <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib53\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib53\" data-open=\"bib53\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2002</a>) and the lens profiles subtracted to leave the lensed image. These lens-subtracted images, along with their corresponding noise maps and point spread functions (PSFs) modelled by <span class=\"small-caps\">tinytim</span> (Krist <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib40\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib40\" data-open=\"bib40\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">1993</a>) are those used by the SLI reconstruction algorithm.</p><p class=\"chapter-para\">The left-hand column in Fig.\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-fig1\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"fig1\" data-open=\"fig1\" class=\"link link-reveal link-table xref-fig\">1</a> shows the resulting <em>F</em>110\u8201<em>W</em> and <em>F</em>160\u8201<em>W</em> images for each system. We applied annular elliptical masks (after PSF convolution), fitted by eye to each image, to include only the lensed image features. This also masks any noisy residuals which remain after the <span class=\"small-caps\">galfit</span> subtraction.</p>                        <a id=\"111789721\" scrollto-destination=\"111789721\"></a>\r\n<div data-id=\"fig1\" class=\"fig fig-section js-fig-section\"><div class=\"label fig-label\">Figure 1.</div><div class=\"graphic-wrap\"><img class=\"content-image\" src=\"https://oup.silverchair-cdn.com/oup/backfile/Content_public/Journal/mnras/440/3/10.1093_mnras_stu305/3/m_stu305fig1a.jpeg?Expires=1611467636&amp;Signature=d7FRtSvUv~2Ch~qwc4ycoR5AXsAeawNG9IxYzNZvidalJ4ZrhzsyYl0Ra0sIyKyIvlbhdxnKCM1ofXDu-b2H2AuF7l5jCwDz-v9fdt47J63wYS2kF8vI8vBKxHMxRX-GhgC8d2r~D1GRD6QKASRgXEj5bmLv7b~xVCj947taPs9e57DYSm0Upn4S7E-pHrtYq73tkmy~cnypSZzqIXD~Z2503UMCgVIdIVG1k4yeOIUCVKHmVEQRW5gO7CCI~dQUW7TUVKq3MyM-ZBMJEmQBZNzBOLi8km1x0NOrTSYs8yC0tUIHgkDNZFuiPcnY9he0QGduvwHm9cnPaRVm7RwM-g__&amp;Key-Pair-Id=APKAIE5G5CRDK6RD3PGA\" alt=\"Lens reconstructions. class=\"graphic-wrap\"><img class=\"content-image\" src=\"https://oup.silverchair-cdn.com/oup/backfile/Content_public/Journal/mnras/440/3/10.1093_mnras_stu305/3/m_stu305fig1b.jpeg?Expires=1611467636&amp;Signature=32uQNXA~5HFtnLe-yxvcEab-VlloI2k8c3gLUfjl9YUYqMNqkE~HlqAWMdzEk9B9eN1-Z45nqz7ZyP2lhN4~a0weKDyKjU6eMEltQvfyD0Sj3gjNsM2NqNXVSL0XyS6A~PyowPNjDYTcJikZb~bqxuKi7ESa5RvgLzN1sx8HBnKxtW-jQS7Mjv9WknZYJc5Pob8FyXHQ4zSd6Jwd~YuKQO0SjcvTkqsjg0z6k~Vp5nsitBMTLxs-iaqX4zix83mSoded5x6bP1rzItYqVR9r8hTdowSMuVDfLWiaoLPe6QVu6qiseh9uT0FbRAb5zanG1U3fSqfOjp6DmQqyW841LQ__&amp;Key-Pair-Id=APKAIE5G5CRDK6RD3PGA\"
