class=\"chapter-para\">\u2013\u2003<em>continued</em></p></div></div>                    <h2 scrollto-destination=111789723 id=\"111789723\" class=\"section-title\" data-legacy-id=sec3>3 METHODOLOGY</h2>\r\n<p class=\"chapter-para\">In this paper, we apply the SLI method originally derived by Warren &amp; Dye (<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib80\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib80\" data-open=\"bib80\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2003</a>). We use the Bayesian version of the SLI method applied by Dye et\u00a0al. (<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib27\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib27\" data-open=\"bib27\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2007</a>, <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib28\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib28\" data-open=\"bib28\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2008</a>), based on the version developed by Suyu et\u00a0al. (<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib64\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib64\" data-open=\"bib64\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2006</a>). In addition, the adaptive source plane grid introduced by Dye &amp; Warren (<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib26\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib26\" data-open=\"bib26\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2005</a>) is used.</p><p class=\"chapter-para\">In this section, we describe an enhancement to the SLI method that allows multiple images to be simultaneously reconstructed using the same lens mass model. Including multiple images in the inversion gives rise to stronger constraints on the lens model parameters. This is particularly true if the images are observed at different wavelengths since colour variations across the lensed source mean that each image probes a different line of sight through the gravitational potential of the lensing galaxy. We describe the modifications necessary for the inclusion of multiple images but refer the reader to the aforementioned papers for more comprehensive details of the underlying SLI method.</p>                    <h3 scrollto-destination=111789726 id=\"111789726\" class=\"section-title\" data-legacy-id=sec3-1>3.1 Multi-image SLI method</h3>\r\n<p class=\"chapter-para\">The SLI method assumes a pixelized image and a pixelized source plane. For a given lens model, the method computes the linear superposition of lensed images of each source plane pixel that best fits the observed lensed image. In the original formulation, the rectangular matrix <em>f</em><sub><em>ij</em></sub> held the fluxes of lensed image pixels <em>j</em> for each source plane pixel <em>i</em> of unit surface brightness. In this way, a model lensed image was created with flux values equal to \u2211<sub><em>i</em></sub>\u8201<em>s</em><sub><em>i</em></sub><em>f</em><sub><em>ij</em></sub> for each image pixel <em>j</em> given source pixel surface brightnesses <em>s</em><sub><em>i</em></sub>. Subtracting this model image from the observed image, which has pixel flux values <em>d</em><sub><em>j</em></sub> and 1\u03c3 uncertainties \u03c3<sub><em>j</em></sub>, allows the \u03c7<sup>2</sup> statistic to be computed.</p><div class=\"&#xA;            block-child-p&#xA;            \">To cope with multiple images, we need to introduce a new index to each of these quantities to denote separate image numbers. The \u03c7<sup>2</sup> statistic in this case becomes <div class=\"formula-wrap\"><div class=\"disp-formula\" id=\"equ1\" content-id=\"equ1\"><div class=\"tex-math display-math\"> \\begin{equation}\r\n\\chi ^2=\\sum _{k=1}^K \\left[ \\sum _{j=1}^{J_k} \\left( \\frac{\\sum _{i=1}^{I_k} s_i^k f_{ij}^k - d_j^k}{\\sigma _j^k}\\right)^2 \\right],\r\n\\end{equation} </div></div><span class=\"label\">(1)</span></div>where there is now an additional sum over images <em>k</em>. Note that each image <em>k</em> has its own source image with surface brightnesses | $s_i^k$ | in pixels <em>i</em>. The image of each of these pixels stored in | $f_{ij}^k$ | must be convolved with the PSF of the observed image <em>k</em>. Also note that each of the <em>k</em> sources and <em>k</em> images can have different numbers of pixels, <em>I</em><sub><em>k</em></sub> and <em>J</em><sub><em>k</em></sub>, respectively.</div><div class=\"&#xA;            block-child-p&#xA;            \">As in the single-image version of the SLI method, the minimum \u03c7<sup>2</sup> solution is given by <div class=\"formula-wrap\"><div class=\"disp-formula\" id=\"equ2\" content-id=\"equ2\"><div class=\"tex-math display-math\"> \\begin{equation}\r\n{\\boldsymbol {s}} = {\\bf F}^{-1} {\\boldsymbol d}\r\n\\end{equation} </div></div><span class=\"label\">(2)</span></div>but now the matrix | ${\\bf F}$ | is a block-diagonal matrix diag(| ${\\bf F}$ |<sup>1</sup>, | ${\\bf F}$ |<sup>2</sup>, \u2026, | ${\\bf F}$ |<sup><em>K</em></sup>) comprising the submatrices | ${\\bf F}$ |<sup><em>k</em></sup> and | ${\\boldsymbol d}$ | is a column vector which itself has column vector elements | ${\\boldsymbol d}^k$ |. The elements of | ${\\bf F}$ |<sup><em>k</em></sup> and | ${\\boldsymbol d}^k$ | are, respectively, <div class=\"formula-wrap\"><div class=\"disp-formula\" id=\"equ3\" content-id=\"equ3\"><div class=\"tex-math display-math\"> \\begin{equation}\r\nF_{ij}^{k}=\\sum _{n=1}^{J_n}\\,f_{\\rm in}^k f_{jn}^k / (\\sigma _n^k)^2 \\, \\,, \\quad d_i^k=\\sum _{n=1}^{J_n}\\,f_{\\rm in}^k d_{n}^k / (\\sigma _n^k)^2 \\,.\r\n\\end{equation} </div></div><span class=\"label\">(3)</span></div>Finally, the column vector | ${\\boldsymbol {s}}$ | contains the source pixel surface brightnesses arranged in order | $s_1^1, s_2^1,\\ldots , s_{I_1}^1, s_1^2,\\ldots , s_{I_K-1}^K, s_{I_K}^K$ |.</div><p class=\"chapter-para\">To regularize the solution, equation <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-equ2\"></span><a href=\"#equ2\" class=\"sectionLink xref-sec\">(2)</a> must be modified by the regularization matrix | ${\\bf H}$ | as described in Warren &amp; Dye (<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib80\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib80\" data-open=\"bib80\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2003</a>). However, in the case of multiple images, | ${\\bf H}$ | becomes the block diagonal matrix diag(\u03bb<sub>1</sub>| ${\\bf H}$ |<sup>1</sup>, \u03bb<sub>2</sub>| ${\\bf H}$ |<sup>2</sup>, \u2026, \u03bb<sub><em>K</em></sub>| ${\\bf H}$ |<sup><em>K</em></sup>), where each submatrix | ${\\bf H}$ |<sup><em>k</em></sup> corresponds to the chosen regularization matrix for source <em>k</em>. Note that in this formalism, each source <em>k</em> is assigned its own independent regularization weight \u03bb<sub><em>k</em></sub>. We regularize each source plane using the scheme described in Dye et\u00a0al. (<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib28\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib28\" data-open=\"bib28\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2008</a>) appropriate for adaptive source grids. In principle, instead of regularizing each source plane independently, different source planes could be allowed to regularize one another in which case | ${\\bf H}$ | would not be block-diagonal. This could be beneficial if the source is expected to be similar between the different input images but would bias the lens model if not.</p><div class=\"&#xA;            block-child-p&#xA;            \">The procedure for finding the most probable lens model parameters then turns to Bayesian inference as described by Dye et\u00a0al. (<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib28\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib28\" data-open=\"bib28\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2008</a>). Adapting equation 7 in Dye et\u00a0al. (<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib28\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib28\" data-open=\"bib28\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2008</a>) to the multi-image case here results in the following expression for the Bayesian evidence, \u03f5: <div class=\"formula-wrap\"><div class=\"disp-formula\" id=\"equ4\" content-id=\"equ4\"><div class=\"tex-math display-math\"> \\begin{eqnarray}\r\n-2 \\,{\\rm ln} \\, \\epsilon &amp;=&amp; \\chi ^2 +{\\rm ln} \\, \\left[ {\\rm det} (\\bf F+\\bf H)\\right] -{\\rm ln} \\, \\left[ {\\rm det} (\\bf H)\\right] \\nonumber \\\\\r\n&amp; &amp; +\\, \\boldsymbol {s}^{{\\rm T}}{\\bf H}\\boldsymbol {s} + \\sum _{k=1}^K\\sum _{j=1}^{J_k} {\\rm ln} \\left[2\\pi (\\sigma _j^k)^2 \\right] \\,\r\n\\end{eqnarray} </div></div><span class=\"label\">(4)</span></div>with \u03c7<sup>2</sup> given by equation <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-equ1\"></span><a href=\"#equ1\" class=\"sectionLink xref-sec\">(1)</a> and where | ${\\bf F}$ |, | ${\\bf H}$ | and | ${\\boldsymbol {s}}$ | are the multi-image quantities defined above. The negative logarithm of the evidence as given above is then minimized by applying the Markov Chain Monte Carlo (MCMC) technique to the lens model parameters (see the next section), the regularization weights<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-fn2\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"fn2\" data-open=\"fn2\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-fn js-xref-fn\"><sup>1</sup></a> and a parameter called the \u2018splitting factor\u2019 which controls the distribution of source plane pixel sizes on the adaptive grid (see Dye et\u00a0al. <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib28\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib28\" data-open=\"bib28\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2008</a>, for more details). After the MCMC chain has burnt in, we allow a further 100\u00a0000 iterations to estimate parameter confidences.</div><p class=\"chapter-para\">We note a further practicality. When computing the \u03c7<sup>2</sup> term in equation <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-equ4\"></span><a href=\"#equ4\" class=\"sectionLink xref-sec\">(4)</a>, we carry out the sum over image pixels contained within an annular mask surrounding the ring. The mask is tailored for each image to include the image of the entire source plane, with minimal extraneous sky. This increases the fraction of significant image pixels with the effect that the evidence is more sensitive to the model parameters.</p><p class=\"chapter-para\">Finally, we point out that the multi-image SLI method as presented assumes that all images are statistically independent of each other. In the case of images that are not statistically independent, for example, as could be the case with image slices in an integral field unit data cube or spectral-line interferometric data cube, equation <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-equ1\"></span><a href=\"#equ1\" class=\"sectionLink xref-sec\">(1)</a> must be modified to include the relevant covariance terms.</p>                    <h3 scrollto-destination=111789734 id=\"111789734\" class=\"section-title\" data-legacy-id=sec3-2>3.2 Lens model</h3>\r\n<p class=\"chapter-para\">We model each of the five lenses considered in this work with a single smooth density profile to describe the distribution of the total (baryonic and dark) lens mass. In order to directly compare with the work of Bolton et\u00a0al. (<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib9\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib9\" data-open=\"bib9\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2012</a>) and Sonnenfeld et\u00a0al. (<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib62\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib62\" data-open=\"bib62\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2013</a>), we use the power-law density profile assumed in these studies. The volume mass density of this profile, \u03c1, scales with radius, <em>r</em>, as \u03c1\u00a0\u221d\u00a0<em>r</em><sup>\u2212\u03b1</sup>. The implicit assumption made with this profile is that the power-law slope, \u03b1, is scale invariant. This assumption appears to be reasonable, at least on the scales probed by strong lensing, since there is no apparent trend in slope with the ratio of Einstein radius to effective radius (Koopmans et\u00a0al. <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib39\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib39\" data-open=\"bib39\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2006</a>; Ruff et\u00a0al. <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib57\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib57\" data-open=\"bib57\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2011</a>).</p><div class=\"&#xA;            block-child-p&#xA;            \">The corresponding projected mass density profile we therefore use in the lens modelling is the elliptical power-law profile introduced by Kassiola &amp; Kovner (<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib37\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib37\" data-open=\"bib37\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">1993</a>), which has a surface mass density, \u03ba, given by <div class=\"formula-wrap\"><div class=\"disp-formula\" id=\"equ5\" content-id=\"equ5\"><div class=\"tex-math display-math\"> \\begin{equation}\r\n\\kappa =\\kappa _0\\,({\\tilde{r}}/{\\rm 1\\,kpc})^{1-\\alpha } \\,,\r\n\\end{equation} </div></div><span class=\"label\">(5)</span></div>where \u03ba<sub>0</sub> is the normalization surface mass density (the special case of \u03b1\u00a0=\u00a02 corresponds to the singular isothermal ellipsoid). The radius | ${\\tilde{r}}$ | is the elliptical radius defined by | ${\\tilde{r}}^2 =x^{\\prime 2}+y^{\\prime 2}/\\epsilon ^2$ |, where \u03f5 is the lens elongation defined as the ratio of the semimajor to semiminor axes. There are three further parameters that describe this profile: the orientation of the semimajor axis measured in a counterclockwise sense from north, \u03b8, and the coordinates of the centre of the lens in the image plane, (<em>x</em><sub>c</sub>, <em>y</em><sub>c</sub>). We also include two further parameters to allow for an external shear field, namely, the shear strength, \u03b3, and shear direction angle, again measured counterclockwise from north, \u03b8<sub>\u03b3</sub>. The shear direction angle is defined to be perpendicular to the direction of resulting image stretch. This brings the total number of lens model parameters to eight. We assume a uniform prior for all eight parameters. In the MCMC contour plots presented in the appendix, we marginalize over (<em>x</em><sub>c</sub>, <em>y</em><sub>c</sub>) since we did not detect any significant offsets between the lens mass centre and the centroid of the lens galaxy light.</div>                    <h2 scrollto-destination=111789737 id=\"111789737\" class=\"section-title\" data-legacy-id=sec4>4 RESULTS</h2>\r\n<div class=\"&#xA;            block-child-p&#xA;            \">The reconstruction of each of the five lenses is shown in Fig.\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-fig1\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"fig1\" data-open=\"fig1\" class=\"link link-reveal link-table xref-fig\">1</a>. Table\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-tbl1\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"tbl1\" data-open=\"tbl1\" class=\"link link-reveal link-table xref-fig\">1</a> gives the lens model parameters, including the geometric average Einstein radius, \u03b8<sub>E</sub>, computed as <div class=\"formula-wrap\"><div class=\"disp-formula\" id=\"equ6\" content-id=\"equ6\"><div class=\"tex-math display-math\"> \\begin{equation}\r\n\\left(\\frac{\\theta _{\\rm E}}{1\\,\\rm kpc}\\right)= \\left(\\frac{2}{3-\\alpha } \\, \\frac{1}{\\sqrt{\\epsilon }} \\, \\frac{\\kappa _o}{\\Sigma _{\\rm CR}}\\right)^{\\frac{1}{\\alpha -1}},\r\n\\end{equation} </div></div><span class=\"label\">(6)</span></div>where \u03a3<sub>CR</sub> is the critical surface mass density (see, for example Schneider, Ehlers &amp; Falco <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib60\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib60\" data-open=\"bib60\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">1992</a>).</div>                        <a id=\"111789739\" scrollto-destination=\"111789739\"></a>\r\n<div content-id=\"tbl1\" class=\"table-modal table-full-width-wrap\"><div class=\"table-wrap table-wide\"><div class=\"table-wrap-title\" id=\"tbl1\" data-id=\"tbl1\"><span class=\"label\">Table 1.</span><div class=\"caption\"><p class=\"chapter-para\">Lens model parameters. Reading from left to right, columns are the H-ATLAS identifier (including the Negrello et\u00a0al. <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib48\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib48\" data-open=\"bib48\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2010</a> identifier), the lens redshift, | $z$ |<sub>d</sub>, the source redshift, | $z$ |<sub>s</sub>, the density profile slope, \u03b1, the lens mass normalization, \u03ba<sub>0</sub> (in units of 10<sup>10</sup>\u00a0M<sub>\u2299</sub>\u00a0kpc<sup>\u22122</sup>), the elongation of the lens mass profile, \u03f5, the orientation of the semimajor axis of the lens, \u03b8, measured counterclockwise from north, the strength of the external shear component, \u03b3, and the Einstein radius, \u03b8<sub>E</sub>, in arcsec computed from equation <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-equ6\"></span><a href=\"#equ6\" class=\"sectionLink xref-sec\">(6)</a>.</p></div> </div><div Reading from left to right, columns are the H-ATLAS identifier (including the Negrello et\u00a0al. <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib48\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib48\" 0.06\u00a0</td></tr></tbody></table></div><div class=\"&#xA;          graphic-wrap&#xA;          \"><a class=\"fig-view-orig openInAnotherWindow btn js-view-large\" target=\"_blank\" href=\"/view-large/111789739\">\r\n          Open in new tab\r\n        </a></div></div></div>                    <h3 scrollto-destination=111789740 id=\"111789740\" class=\"section-title\" data-legacy-id=sec4-1>4.1 Object notes</h3>\r\n<p class=\"chapter-para\">In this section, we detail the characteristics of each lens system. In particular, we compare with the results of Bussmann et\u00a0al. (<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib14\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib14\" data-open=\"bib14\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2013</a>, <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib14\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib14\" data-open=\"bib14\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">B13</a> hereafter) who have modelled imaging data acquired with the Submillimeter Array (SMA) for \u223c30 candidate lenses discovered by H-ATLAS and HerMES. All five of the H-ATLAS SDP lenses are modelled by <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib14\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib14\" data-open=\"bib14\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">B13</a>, although we point out that external shear is not included in their lens model.</p><p class=\"chapter-para\"><em>J090311.6+003906</em> (ID81). This is a classic cusp\u2013caustic configuration lens. The near-IR emission in the lensed image closely matches that of the SMA data and unsurprisingly results in a consistent magnification. The reconstructed source in both <em>HST</em> filters shows little structure other than a slight elongation along the NE\u2013SW direction. The centroid of the source is well aligned with that found by <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib14\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib14\" data-open=\"bib14\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">B13</a>. The lens model is an excellent fit to the observed data and leaves no significant residuals. The model requires a small amount of external shear with strength \u03b3 \u2243 0.05 and direction \u03b8<sub>\u03b3</sub> \u2243 105\u00b0, consistent with perturbations expected from a nearby group of galaxies to the east. The elongation and orientation of the required model is entirely consistent with that of the observed light profile.</p><p class=\"chapter-para\"><em>J090740.0\u2212004200</em> (ID9). This lens system is dominated by a single doubly imaged source with a simple morphology lying to the north of the lens galaxy centroid. The double imaging is consistent with the SMA data but the emission appears to originate from a different location in the source compared to what is observed in the near-IR <em>HST</em> data. The reconstructed source <em>F</em>110\u8201<em>W</em>\u2013<em>F</em>160\u8201<em>W</em> colour map shows a reddening gradient which points from the near-IR source towards the SMA source centroid. Some of the fainter emission from the source in the near-IR crosses the caustic and gives rise to the observed complete Einstein ring. There is also some fainter structure in the ring which is fit in the model by a fainter source to the east of the lens centroid. Negligible external shear is required in the best-fitting lens model. The modest elongation of \u03f5\u00a0=\u00a01.14 indicates a more radially symmetric mass profile compared to the light. The mass and light are significantly misaligned in this lens (see Section\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-sec4-4\"></span><a href=\"#sec4-4\" class=\"sectionLink xref-sec\">4.4</a>).</p><p class=\"chapter-para\"><em>J091043.1\u2212000321</em> (ID11). Like ID9, this system has a complete Einstein ring. The ring's significant ellipticity is the result of a relatively strong external shear field of strength \u03b3\u00a0=\u00a00.23. The direction of this shear points almost exactly to the centre of a nearby edge-on spiral galaxy to the NW located at a redshift of | $z$ |\u00a0=\u00a00.39\u00a0\u00b1\u00a00.09 (see <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib49a\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib49a\" data-open=\"bib49a\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">N14</a>). The implication is therefore that this spiral is almost entirely responsible for the shear perturbation. We attempted a model where external shear was replaced by a second singular isothermal lens to represent the spiral's total mass but found no significant improvement in the fit. The reconstructed source exhibits clear small-scale structure in both filters, required to fit the observed structure in the ring. The majority of the emission observed in the ring comes from a doubly imaged source lying just outside the caustic and the smaller scale structure comes from smaller knots of emission in the source, some of which are quadruply imaged. As the residual plot in Fig.\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-fig1\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"fig1\" data-open=\"fig1\" class=\"link link-reveal link-table xref-fig\">1</a> indicates, the model image does not perfectly account for the observed features in the ring and this is also reflected in the fact that the model fit is only marginally acceptable. It is therefore possible that some of the ring structure is actually structure in the lens galaxy not fully removed by <span class=\"small-caps\">galfit</span>. The SMA data imply a doubly imaged source which, like J090740.0\u2212004200, is offset from the near-IR emission but has a comparable Einstein radius. As with ID9, the reconstructed source <em>F</em>110\u8201<em>W</em>\u2013<em>F</em>160\u8201<em>W</em> colour map shows a reddening gradient which points from the near-IR source towards the SMA source centroid.</p><p class=\"chapter-para\">In addition to the reconstructed near-IR source presented here, there appears to be further lensed arcs associated with this system (not shown in Fig.\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-fig1\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"fig1\" data-open=\"fig1\" class=\"link link-reveal link-table xref-fig\">1</a>). Two readily identified arcs lie to the south and to the east of the lens centre and trace out a ring with an Einstein radius larger than that shown in Fig.\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-fig1\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"fig1\" data-open=\"fig1\" class=\"link link-reveal link-table xref-fig\">1</a>. This implies that the lensed source responsible lies at a different redshift. We chose not to complicate the lens model further by incorporating this additional source but instead leave this for further work.</p><p class=\"chapter-para\"><em>J091305.0\u2212005343</em> (ID130). The lens galaxy is relatively poorly constrained in this system. The best-fitting model is consistent with zero external shear (as might be expected from the lack of observed nearby perturbers) and the lens has one of the higher elongations found in the sample of \u03f5\u00a0=\u00a01.34. The best-fitting reconstructed source is very extended and as such, the magnification is low. The residual map shown in Fig.\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-fig1\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"fig1\" data-open=\"fig1\" class=\"link link-reveal link-table xref-fig\">1</a> shows some significant features towards the lens centre which is not surprising given the difficulty reported by <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib49a\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib49a\" data-open=\"bib49a\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">N14</a> in removing the lens light. However, the residuals contribute an insignificant amount of light to the overall source and as such will have a negligible effect on the lens model parameters. The SMA data are in close agreement with the near-IR data which increases confidence in the <span class=\"small-caps\">galfit</span> subtraction (see <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib49a\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib49a\" data-open=\"bib49a\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">N14</a>), although the near-IR data imply a slightly larger magnification than the SMA data. We make the caveat that this lens, as pointed out by <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib49a\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib49a\" data-open=\"bib49a\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">N14</a>, is a likely Sa galaxy. In order to compare our results with those of the aforementioned lensing studies which include only early-type lenses, we have omitted this system from our fits to the trends reported later in this paper.</p><p class=\"chapter-para\"><em>J090302.9\u2212014127</em> (ID17). This is a relatively poorly constrained lens although the fit is acceptable. There are some minor features in the residual image which occur at 3 o'clock and 11 o'clock around the inner radius of the annulus as shown in Fig.\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-fig1\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"fig1\" data-open=\"fig1\" class=\"link link-reveal link-table xref-fig\">1</a>. <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib49a\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib49a\" data-open=\"bib49a\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">N14</a> have modelled the flux in the lensed image by fitting individual <span class=\"small-caps\">galfit</span> profiles to the different components. Two of these profiles lie on the inner radius of the annulus and these coincide with the locations of the residuals found in the lens modelling here. One interpretation is therefore that spatially dependent extinction in the lensing galaxy affects the lensed image. This is consistent with the colour of the feature at 11 o'clock which <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib49a\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib49a\" data-open=\"bib49a\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">N14</a> determine as having a significantly redder colour than the average colour of the other <span class=\"small-caps\">galfit</span> profiles, but not with the feature at 3 o'clock which is consistent in colour. An alternative explanation might therefore be that the lensed image contains residual flux from the lensing galaxy itself which is highly possible given the complexity in removing the lens from this system. Nevertheless, the reconstructed source plane shows two very prominent elongated objects in both bands which converge at a point interior to the caustic. The majority of this source plane emission is doubly imaged but some of the flux in the merged region is quadruply imaged. The fact that the lensed image is well fitted by a relatively simple source surface brightness map adds reassurance that the reconstruction is plausible; an over-complicated source often indicates that there are non-lensed features in the lensed image. Although the SMA data for this lens are unable to resolve individual ring features, <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib14\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib14\" data-open=\"bib14\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">B13</a> obtain a magnification consistent with that measured from the near-IR data here.</p>                    <h3 scrollto-destination=111789748 id=\"111789748\" class=\"section-title\" data-legacy-id=sec4-2>4.2 Mass profile versus redshift</h3>\r\n<p class=\"chapter-para\">Fig.\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-fig2\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"fig2\" data-open=\"fig2\" class=\"link link-reveal link-table xref-fig\">2</a> shows the fitted lens density profile slopes plotted against redshift. Discounting the likely Sa lens J091305.0\u2212005343, the straight line minimum \u03c7<sup>2</sup> fit through the four data points is \u03b1\u00a0=\u00a02.05 \u00b1 0.08\u00a0\u2212\u00a0(0.30 \u00b1 0.13)| $z$ |. (If we include this fifth lens, the fit becomes \u03b1\u00a0=\u00a02.01 \u00b1 0.10\u00a0\u2212\u00a0(0.25 \u00b1 0.15)| $z$ |.) The dotted line and grey shaded envelope in Fig.\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-fig2\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"fig2\" data-open=\"fig2\" class=\"link link-reveal link-table xref-fig\">2</a> shows the fit and the 1\u03c3 error region, respectively. In the same figure, we plot the variation in slope with redshift from three other lens sample combinations: (1) the SL2S lens sample of \u03b1\u00a0=\u00a02.05 \u00b1 0.06\u00a0\u2212\u00a0(0.13 \u00b1 0.24)| $z$ | from Sonnenfeld et\u00a0al. (<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib62\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib62\" data-open=\"bib62\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2013</a>), (2) the combination of the Lensing Structure and Dynamics sample of Treu &amp; Koopmans (<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib69\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib69\" data-open=\"bib69\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2004</a>), SL2S and SLACS of \u03b1\u00a0=\u00a02.08 \u00b1 0.02\u00a0\u2212\u00a0(0.31 \u00b1 0.10)| $z$ | also from Sonnenfeld et\u00a0al. (<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib62\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib62\" data-open=\"bib62\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2013</a>) and (3) the combination of SLACS and BOSS of \u03b1\u00a0= 2.11 \u00b1 0.02\u00a0\u2212\u00a0(0.60 \u00b1 0.15)| $z$ | from Bolton et\u00a0al. (<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib9\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib9\" data-open=\"bib9\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2012</a>).</p>                        <a id=\"111789750\" scrollto-destination=\"111789750\"></a>\r\n<div data-id=\"fig2\" class=\"fig fig-section js-fig-section\"><div class=\"label fig-label\">Figure 2.</div><div class=\"graphic-wrap\"><img class=\"content-image\" src=\"https://oup.silverchair-cdn.com/oup/backfile/Content_public/Journal/mnras/440/3/10.1093_mnras_stu305/3/m_stu305fig2.jpeg?Expires=1611467636&amp;Signature=omP9lLobm1JkuOVFvEqhkfe~gdYtR1YNn-7w3MZTrn6DhZVyddGLS0ij37RGtv~AtWSM2H2FTwpKPjdvoIiu0WzLwm66W3Npn3VCxeSkAtKIjXuYV86H9Zj1UZgX0isjsCPU6XoldAzFz44CpOhNMExFNZupgg5rkhZZu7b6NW5q6DqSgiC2j21SRNlEcy~YfSOojaVopCNNFrcbi642Z9XzkxsoxK4LXHh1VkajrSJLk9qzukrT7ZJfFNjVtGxb8tyh0-rG5uYvgqW6mK6koWK1o~RKi7-vKVbDKz0INcP194j2fzQld1jO2wHDl6VSotIxfc8RtV5dJ53EDGT5IQ__&amp;Key-Pair-Id=APKAIE5G5CRDK6RD3PGA\" alt=\"Variation of the density profile slope, \u03b1, as a function of the lens redshift,  $z$ d. This is perhaps not surprising given the small sample size presently at our disposal. This limitation will be considerably reduced by our forthcoming rapidly growing lens sample.</p>                    <h3 scrollto-destination=111789752 id=\"111789752\" class=\"section-title\" data-legacy-id=sec4-3>4.3 Lens magnifications</h3>\r\n<p class=\"chapter-para\">Table\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-tbl2\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"tbl2\" data-open=\"tbl2\" class=\"link link-reveal link-table xref-fig\">2</a> lists the source flux magnifications. For each lens, we have computed the magnification at every point in the MCMC chain as presented in Fig.\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-fig6\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"fig6\" data-open=\"fig6\" class=\"link link-reveal link-table xref-fig\">A1</a> to form a magnification distribution.     \"><a class=\"fig-view-orig openInAnotherWindow btn js-view-large\" target=\"_blank\" href=\"/view-large/111789754\">\r\n          Open in new tab\r\n        </a></div></div></div><p class=\"chapter-para\">We computed magnifications using the higher signal-to-noise <em>F</em>160\u8201<em>W</em> data to give more precise magnifications. We find that the magnifications computed using the <em>F</em>110\u8201<em>W</em> data generally have a larger spread but the distribution is always consistent with those computed using the <em>F</em>160\u8201<em>W</em> data.</p><p class=\"chapter-para\">We determined three different magnifications to give an indication of the strength of near-IR differential amplification. The first is a \u2018total magnification\u2019, \u03bc<sub>tot</sub>, computed as the ratio of the total flux in the masked region of the image as shown in Fig.\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-fig1\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"fig1\" data-open=\"fig1\" class=\"link link-reveal link-table xref-fig\">1</a> to the total flux in the source plane. The second and third, \u03bc<sub>0.5</sub> and \u03bc<sub>0.1</sub>, correspond to the magnification of the brightest region(s) of the source that contributes 50 and 10\u00a0per\u8201cent, respectively, of the total reconstructed source flux. Note that \u03bc<sub>tot</sub> is almost always lower than \u03bc<sub>0.5</sub> and \u03bc<sub>0.1</sub> since incorporating the total source plane typically includes additional regions that are less magnified.</p><p class=\"chapter-para\">Table\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-tbl2\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"tbl2\" data-open=\"tbl2\" class=\"link link-reveal link-table xref-fig\">2</a> also lists the magnifications, \u03bc<sub>880</sub>, determined by <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib14\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib14\" data-open=\"bib14\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">B13</a> at 880\u00a0| $\\mu$ |m. These magnifications are calculated in an elliptical disc centred on the best-fitting 880\u00a0| $\\mu$ |m source brightness profile with a radius twice that containing half of the total source flux. \u03bc<sub>880</sub> is the ratio of the integrated flux within the image plane region mapped by this disc to the integrated flux within the disc in the source plane itself. For the S\u00e9rsic profiles fit to the SDP sources by <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib14\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib14\" data-open=\"bib14\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">B13</a>, this corresponds to approximately 75\u00a0per\u8201cent of the source light in all cases. Therefore, \u03bc<sub>880</sub> corresponds to a magnification somewhere between \u03bc<sub>tot</sub> and \u03bc<sub>0.5</sub>.</p><p class=\"chapter-para\">As Table\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-tbl2\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"tbl2\" data-open=\"tbl2\" class=\"link link-reveal link-table xref-fig\">2</a> shows, for all lenses apart from J091305.0\u2212005343, \u03bc<sub>880</sub> is consistent with a value spanned by \u03bc<sub>tot</sub> and \u03bc<sub>0.5</sub>. The consistency is generally better when the 880| $\\,\\mu$ |m source morphology more closely resembles the reconstructed near-IR source. This is a reflection of the fact that the lens models determined at both wavelengths are geometrically similar.<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-fn3\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"fn3\" data-open=\"fn3\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-fn js-xref-fn\"><sup>2</sup></a> The exception is J091305.0\u2212005343 which has a very similar source morphology between 880| $\\,\\mu$ |m and the near-IR, but in this case, the magnification discrepancy is brought about by a significantly different lens elongation, with the near-IR lens model favouring an elongation of \u223c1.3 compared to \u223c2.0 at 880| $\\,\\mu$ |m.</p>                    <h3 scrollto-destination=111789759 id=\"111789759\" class=\"section-title\" data-legacy-id=sec4-4>4.4 Comparison of mass and light morphology</h3>\r\n<p class=\"chapter-para\">A question which brings insight to models of galaxy formation and evolution is how closely the visible mass traces the dark matter halo. One way to address this is to compare the visible morphology of the lens galaxies to the total mass profiles determined through lensing.</p><p class=\"chapter-para\">We used the <span class=\"small-caps\">galfit</span> models of <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib49a\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib49a\" data-open=\"bib49a\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">N14</a> to determine the elongation and orientation of the lens galaxy surface brightness profiles. Table\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-tbl3\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"tbl3\" data-open=\"tbl3\" class=\"link link-reveal link-table xref-fig\">3</a> lists these along with the effective radii. In the case of J091305.0\u2212005343 (ID130), we used only the light profiles which make up the bulge, since the bulge comprises nearly all of the light and, unlike the faint disc, has a coherent set of profiles which give a well-defined orientation and elongation.</p>                        <a id=\"111789762\" scrollto-destination=\"111789762\"></a>\r\n<div content-id=\"tbl3\" class=\"table-modal table-full-width-wrap\"><div class=\"table-wrap table-wide\"><div class=\"table-wrap-title\" id=\"tbl3\" data-id=\"tbl3\"><span class=\"label\">Table 3.</span><div class=\"caption\"><p class=\"chapter-para\">Morphology of the lens light profile and lens masses. Columns are: elongation, \u03f5<sub>light</sub>, position angle (measured counterclockwise from north), \u03b8<sub>light</sub>, effective radius, <em>R</em><sub>e</sub>, the stellar mass contained within a radius of <em>R</em><sub>e</sub>/2 for a Salpeter and Chabrier IMF, | $M^{\\rm *,Salp}_{R_{\\rm e}/2}$ | and | $M^{\\rm *,Chab}_{R_{\\rm e}/2}$ |, respectively (derived from the stellar masses computed in <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib49a\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib49a\" data-open=\"bib49a\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">N14</a>) and the total mass within a radius of <em>R</em><sub>e</sub>/2 inferred from the lens model, | $M^{\\rm Tot}_{R_{\\rm e}/2}$ |. All masses are in units of 10<sup>10</sup>\u8201M<sub>\u2299</sub>. Note that the light profile parameters for J091305.0\u2212005343 refer only to the bulge component and exclude the faint disc.</p></div> </div><div This implies that the dark matter halo is comparable in elongation or rounder in each case.</p>                        <a id=\"111789764\" scrollto-destination=\"111789764\"></a>\r\n<div data-id=\"fig3\" class=\"fig fig-section js-fig-section\"><div class=\"label fig-label\">Figure 3.</div><div class=\"graphic-wrap\"><img class=\"content-image\" src=\"https://oup.silverchair-cdn.com/oup/backfile/Content_public/Journal/mnras/440/3/10.1093_mnras_stu305/3/m_stu305fig3.jpeg?Expires=1611467636&amp;Signature=S3gIEny0lDOL684Jsm12qKOOk3tw9SKGb7QFAXlT4havc4KL93ZcHHyiEhlHjurzHb-9WX8M0~vrSP2krwPUBhM~Dx9uIzOvba2ATULGSftLYwhpwx3UGBHl3rSjxAZqiaEPQOrsPtKrWuxRNxzZ6bjI~NcRrnZ7LVGaBTvMRapZkyidTrKLFT3W9dX3aVkkhSzAnOUHp6q38iwG9KKbnoocY2VIqY8mM6~-sm1eQKK-PFUJTNo5Z038EDeFE6CeDoPEynjPycLpQ3JPhRwsJChqpv-3b09lxIqGaJB5mWKhsBndDv0fVHRqWM5fKW0bbCZpHafXjaq-irOxXWOWmA__&amp;Key-Pair-Id=APKAIE5G5CRDK6RD3PGA\" alt=\"Comparison of light with mass. Top panel: elongation of lens mass profile versus elongation of observed light. Bottom panel: difference in position angle of lens profile and observed light versus elongation of observed light. In both panels, the grey data point corresponds to the Sa lens J091305.0\u2212005343.\" data-path-from-xml=\"stu305fig3.jpeg\" /><div class=\"fig-orig original-slide\"><a class=\"fig-view-orig js-view-large openInAnotherWindow\" href=\"/view-large/figure/111789764/stu305fig3.jpeg\" data-path-from-xml=\"stu305fig3.jpeg\" target=\"_blank\">Open in new tab</a><a data-section=\"111789764\" href=\"/DownloadFile/DownloadImage.aspx?image=https://oup.silverchair-cdn.com/oup/backfile/Content_public/Journal/mnras/440/3/10.1093_mnras_stu305/3/stu305fig3.jpeg?Expires=1611467636&Signature=H1y8X1DtWlDzANnzmLIzkhGBlhBzKTbY~D9ilGUztlAuFnYqBAXoVMnWWJzdIO5HfzNV3MN3T2dXazh2PRt1beKNBceoTAhwgeAqKkzdpzMMaGCOFS2oWLPiVsA4gdhkwPefdtZ4X1OP1Ir9ajKDF8DfftQeSct0C~5EvGbbVK2Ios79cPRpGETaloVv7ZtgpDIb3hhR0L5qiJvV842XwjqE3ZU0XW-JryiG2xjPqBrrV2lv3YVzI-Bfzf9mW9wk-m7q7pXKq13wS9mdvYtXEo7O8g~yqDJC4oz8bB5iTMSrfg3tkoloHyuSzo622nMBT1h4jTbsh-2Sys16BuJGlw__&Key-Pair-Id=APKAIE5G5CRDK6RD3PGA&sec=111789764&ar=1074641&xsltPath=~/UI/app/XSLT&imagename=&siteId=5326\" class=\"download-slide\" data-path-from-xml=\"stu305fig3.jpeg\">Download slide</a></div></div><div class=\"caption fig-caption\"><p class=\"chapter-para\">Comparison of light with mass. <em>Top panel</em>: elongation of lens mass profile versus elongation of observed light. <em>Bottom panel</em>: difference in position angle of lens profile and observed light versus elongation of observed light. In both panels, the grey data point corresponds to the Sa lens J091305.0\u2212005343.</p></div></div><div content-id=\"fig3\" class=\"fig fig-modal reveal-modal\"><div class=\"label fig-label\">Figure 3.</div><div class=\"graphic-wrap\"><img class=\"content-image\" src=\"https://oup.silverchair-cdn.com/oup/backfile/Content_public/Journal/mnras/440/3/10.1093_mnras_stu305/3/m_stu305fig3.jpeg?Expires=1611467636&amp;Signature=S3gIEny0lDOL684Jsm12qKOOk3tw9SKGb7QFAXlT4havc4KL93ZcHHyiEhlHjurzHb-9WX8M0~vrSP2krwPUBhM~Dx9uIzOvba2ATULGSftLYwhpwx3UGBHl3rSjxAZqiaEPQOrsPtKrWuxRNxzZ6bjI~NcRrnZ7LVGaBTvMRapZkyidTrKLFT3W9dX3aVkkhSzAnOUHp6q38iwG9KKbnoocY2VIqY8mM6~-sm1eQKK-PFUJTNo5Z038EDeFE6CeDoPEynjPycLpQ3JPhRwsJChqpv-3b09lxIqGaJB5mWKhsBndDv0fVHRqWM5fKW0bbCZpHafXjaq-irOxXWOWmA__&amp;Key-Pair-Id=APKAIE5G5CRDK6RD3PGA\" alt=\"Comparison of light with mass. Top panel: elongation of lens mass profile versus elongation of observed light. Bottom panel: difference in position angle of lens profile and observed light versus elongation of observed light. In both panels, the grey data point corresponds to the Sa lens J091305.0\u2212005343.\" data-path-from-xml=\"stu305fig3.jpeg\" /><div class=\"fig-orig original-slide\"><a class=\"fig-view-orig js-view-large openInAnotherWindow\" href=\"/view-large/figure/111789764/stu305fig3.jpeg\" data-path-from-xml=\"stu305fig3.jpeg\" target=\"_blank\">Open in new tab</a><a data-section=\"111789764\" href=\"/DownloadFile/DownloadImage.aspx?image=https://oup.silverchair-cdn.com/oup/backfile/Content_public/Journal/mnras/440/3/10.1093_mnras_stu305/3/stu305fig3.jpeg?Expires=1611467636&Signature=H1y8X1DtWlDzANnzmLIzkhGBlhBzKTbY~D9ilGUztlAuFnYqBAXoVMnWWJzdIO5HfzNV3MN3T2dXazh2PRt1beKNBceoTAhwgeAqKkzdpzMMaGCOFS2oWLPiVsA4gdhkwPefdtZ4X1OP1Ir9ajKDF8DfftQeSct0C~5EvGbbVK2Ios79cPRpGETaloVv7ZtgpDIb3hhR0L5qiJvV842XwjqE3ZU0XW-JryiG2xjPqBrrV2lv3YVzI-Bfzf9mW9wk-m7q7pXKq13wS9mdvYtXEo7O8g~yqDJC4oz8bB5iTMSrfg3tkoloHyuSzo622nMBT1h4jTbsh-2Sys16BuJGlw__&Key-Pair-Id=APKAIE5G5CRDK6RD3PGA&sec=111789764&ar=1074641&xsltPath=~/UI/app/XSLT&imagename=&siteId=5326\" class=\"download-slide\" data-path-from-xml=\"stu305fig3.jpeg\">Download slide</a></div></div><div class=\"caption fig-caption\"><p class=\"chapter-para\">Comparison of light with mass. <em>Top panel</em>: elongation of lens mass profile versus elongation of observed light. <em>Bottom panel</em>: difference in position angle of lens profile and observed light versus elongation of observed light. In both panels, the grey data point corresponds to the Sa lens J091305.0\u2212005343.</p></div></div><p class=\"chapter-para\">The bottom panel of Fig.\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-fig3\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"fig3\" data-open=\"fig3\" class=\"link link-reveal link-table xref-fig\">3</a> compares the offset in orientation of the lens mass and light profiles. Here, there are some significant discrepancies. The offsets are substantially higher on average than those found by SLACS, who measured an rms scatter of 10\u00b0 (Koopmans et\u00a0al. <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib39\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib39\" data-open=\"bib39\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2006</a>), but consistent with the findings of SL2S (Gavazzi et\u00a0al. <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib31\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib31\" data-open=\"bib31\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2012</a>) who measure an rms scatter of 25\u00b0 with offsets of up to 50\u00b0. As Gavazzi et\u00a0al. (<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib31\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib31\" data-open=\"bib31\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2012</a>) point out, the SL2S lenses have a higher average ratio of Einstein radius to effective radius than the SLACS lenses, and hence, the SLACS lenses are more dominated by the stellar component. The average of this ratio for our lenses is even higher than that of the SL2S sample and so we would expect even less alignment between the dark and visible components. We will be able to explore this trend more properly with our forthcoming larger lens sample, although there are already indications from simulations that such large (and even larger) morphological differences between baryons and dark matter are commonplace (for example, see Bett et\u00a0al. <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib6\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib6\" data-open=\"bib6\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2010</a>; Skibba &amp; Macci\u00f2 <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib61\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib61\" data-open=\"bib61\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2011</a>).</p>                    <h3 scrollto-destination=111789766 id=\"111789766\" class=\"section-title\" data-legacy-id=sec4-5>4.5 Other observational trends</h3>\r\n<p class=\"chapter-para\">An important observational benchmark for models of galaxy and structure formation is the fraction of dark matter contained within a fixed fraction of the effective radius. Using the SLACS lens sample, Auger et\u00a0al. (<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib3\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib3\" data-open=\"bib3\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2010b</a>) measure an average projected fraction of dark matter within half the effective radius of 0.21 with a scatter of 0.20, for a Salpeter (<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib58\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib58\" data-open=\"bib58\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">1955</a>) IMF, or 0.55 with a scatter of 0.11 for a Chabrier (<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib17\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib17\" data-open=\"bib17\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2003</a>) IMF. Ruff et\u00a0al. (<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib57\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib57\" data-open=\"bib57\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2011</a>) measure the average of this fraction to be 0.42 with a scatter of 0.20 for a Salpeter IMF for the SL2S lenses.</p><p class=\"chapter-para\">Table\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-tbl3\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"tbl3\" data-open=\"tbl3\" class=\"link link-reveal link-table xref-fig\">3</a> lists the stellar masses and the total lensing projected mass contained within half the effective radius for our lenses. To obtain these stellar masses, we used the total stellar masses and the <span class=\"small-caps\">galfit</span> profiles determined for the lens galaxies by <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib49a\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib49a\" data-open=\"bib49a\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">N14</a>. Excluding the Sa lens J091305.0\u2212005343, our lenses have a mean projected dark matter fraction within half the effective radius of <em>f</em><sub>DM</sub>\u00a0=\u00a00.46 with a scatter of 0.10, for a Salpeter IMF, (<em>f</em><sub>DM</sub>\u00a0=\u00a00.69 with a scatter of 0.07, for a Chabrier IMF). Although this is statistically consistent with the SLACS and SL2S lenses, the higher value measured in both our lenses and the SL2S lenses compared to the SLACS sample most likely reflects the lower average ratio of Einstein radius to effective radius in SLACS.</p><p class=\"chapter-para\">One of the trends detected by the SLACS survey is that <em>f</em><sub>DM</sub> for early types increases with galaxy mass and galaxy size. Auger et\u00a0al. (<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib3\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib3\" data-open=\"bib3\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2010b</a>) measure the linear fit | $f_{\\rm DM}=-0.13\\pm 0.09 + (0.49\\pm 0.10)\\log ({R_{\\rm e}/1\\,{\\rm kpc}})$ |. However, this result is not confirmed by the SL2S lens sample; Ruff et\u00a0al. (<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib57\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib57\" data-open=\"bib57\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2011</a>) fail to find any correlation between <em>f</em><sub>DM</sub> and | $R_{\\rm e}$ | with a measured gradient of | ${\\rm d}f_{\\rm DM}/{{\\rm d}R_{\\rm e}}=0.08^{+0.10}_{-0.08}$ |\u00a0kpc<sup>\u22121</sup>. Our lens sample, excluding the Sa lens J091305.0\u2212005343 gives a linear fit of | $f_{\\rm DM}=-0.01\\pm 0.20 + (1.04\\pm 0.46)\\log ({R_{\\rm e}/1\\,{\\rm kpc}})$ |, steeper but consistent with the SLACS lenses.</p><p class=\"chapter-para\">The SLACS and SL2S lenses also exhibit the trend that the slope of the density profile inferred from lensing is negatively correlated with effective radius and positively correlated with the average surface mass density contained within | ${R_{\\rm e}}/2$ |. In Fig.\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-fig4\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"fig4\" data-open=\"fig4\" class=\"link link-reveal link-table xref-fig\">4</a>, we plot these correlations for our lenses. The top panel shows the density profile slope, \u03b1, plotted against effective radius. For our four early-type lenses, we obtain a straight line fit of | $\\alpha =2.17\\pm 0.20 - (0.70\\pm 0.47)\\log ({R_{\\rm e}/1\\,{\\rm kpc}})$ |, as shown in the figure by the dotted line and grey shaded 1\u03c3 error envelope. This compares to the fit | $\\alpha =2.39\\pm 0.10 - (0.41\\pm 0.12)\\log ({R_{\\rm e}/1\\,{\\rm kpc}})$ | by Auger et\u00a0al. (<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib3\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib3\" data-open=\"bib3\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2010b</a>) for the SLACS lenses and | $\\alpha =2.05\\pm 0.06 - (0.67\\pm 0.20)\\log ({R_{\\rm e}/1\\,{\\rm kpc}})$ | by Sonnenfeld et\u00a0al. (<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib62\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib62\" data-open=\"bib62\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2013</a>) for the SL2S lenses.<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-fn4\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"fn4\" data-open=\"fn4\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-fn js-xref-fn\"><sup>3</sup></a></p>                        <a id=\"111789771\" scrollto-destination=\"111789771\"></a>\r\n<div data-id=\"fig4\" class=\"fig fig-section js-fig-section\"><div class=\"label fig-label\">Figure 4.</div><div class=\"graphic-wrap\"><img class=\"content-image\" src=\"https://oup.silverchair-cdn.com/oup/backfile/Content_public/Journal/mnras/440/3/10.1093_mnras_stu305/3/m_stu305fig4.jpeg?Expires=1611467636&amp;Signature=nUlHd-w3Tq~PH5r-xPCziMtzQucm02gPrEAaOvxhDwg5UCfVdmywBrLOvOSmw5HYH~h9KzHoeNxbVl7Te35PDUPvNcS7R3FudP5ohboNgxw37wcCezZD463JzaqAG-oKQTpwrSczvlO~str8b1aIN5p6ZgpWHZBpOkSqtBX6tFlPRGfyAPb~rUr6n1FI-zIoZDpaZAu7WJhZiyvsrtFXHovQJ~P~FoV0TFE8QGdLvim5-jroHOunQxgyBOvToo~DyfdkRnSa36N4VmztlT6BsJiIHfIeIbM9Wc9vHNevhjPqva8P6IwXzeDnDZBeI87BFwpUi5tMiJ1fwVe3Gl4gFA__&amp;Key-Pair-Id=APKAIE5G5CRDK6RD3PGA\" alt=\"Correlation between total mass density profile slope, \u03b1, and effective radius, Re, (top panel) and average projected total surface mass density within Re/2 (bottom panel). In both panels, the Sa lens J091305.0\u2212005343, coloured with a grey data point, has been omitted in the straight line fit which is shown by the dotted line and grey shaded 1\u03c3 error envelope.\" data-path-from-xml=\"stu305fig4.jpeg\" /><div class=\"fig-orig original-slide\"><a class=\"fig-view-orig js-view-large openInAnotherWindow\" href=\"/view-large/figure/111789771/stu305fig4.jpeg\" data-path-from-xml=\"stu305fig4.jpeg\" target=\"_blank\">Open in new tab</a><a data-section=\"111789771\" href=\"/DownloadFile/DownloadImage.aspx?image=https://oup.silverchair-cdn.com/oup/backfile/Content_public/Journal/mnras/440/3/10.1093_mnras_stu305/3/stu305fig4.jpeg?Expires=1611467636&Signature=HIE64J9QaEBCSkJPZU1B9~Xjgw-tipaGc-EpMAgyV0tdFY3pCMjuD-MiV9bATqHtH20UP21B2HSOgrrlno5kyWwmf50D90TNh6a3FoWY2kPKYJMozFGmLm9M~u8~u4ztmmyJJSuYoC14PooJdGwlVEKnkJCCjGcWvh8i5UHvW61b7BTNqx9QcqXxMqcequOgh2wJ87B5B5~tyMKZXKc2y-PNdcTqj41oAlKGjhwIYWIAg7XmkvZzz1524XGF9q-ZaAHY1Ah9qWSsgr2YuYerP9v8wtTdCLUbfeG7dOFDSUTibHC1UIKEvUllSzgJqWPik~72r1A~qt4bUC71hm5X2Q__&Key-Pair-Id=APKAIE5G5CRDK6RD3PGA&sec=111789771&ar=1074641&xsltPath=~/UI/app/XSLT&imagename=&siteId=5326\" class=\"download-slide\" data-path-from-xml=\"stu305fig4.jpeg\">Download slide</a></div></div><div class=\"caption fig-caption\"><p class=\"chapter-para\">Correlation between total mass density profile slope, \u03b1, and effective radius, <em>R</em><sub>e</sub>, (<em>top panel</em>) and average projected total surface mass density within <em>R</em><sub>e</sub>/2 (<em>bottom panel</em>). In both panels, the Sa lens J091305.0\u2212005343, coloured with a grey data point, has been omitted in the straight line fit which is shown by the dotted line and grey shaded 1\u03c3 error envelope.</p></div></div><div content-id=\"fig4\" class=\"fig fig-modal reveal-modal\"><div class=\"label fig-label\">Figure 4.</div><div class=\"graphic-wrap\"><img class=\"content-image\" src=\"https://oup.silverchair-cdn.com/oup/backfile/Content_public/Journal/mnras/440/3/10.1093_mnras_stu305/3/m_stu305fig4.jpeg?Expires=1611467636&amp;Signature=nUlHd-w3Tq~PH5r-xPCziMtzQucm02gPrEAaOvxhDwg5UCfVdmywBrLOvOSmw5HYH~h9KzHoeNxbVl7Te35PDUPvNcS7R3FudP5ohboNgxw37wcCezZD463JzaqAG-oKQTpwrSczvlO~str8b1aIN5p6ZgpWHZBpOkSqtBX6tFlPRGfyAPb~rUr6n1FI-zIoZDpaZAu7WJhZiyvsrtFXHovQJ~P~FoV0TFE8QGdLvim5-jroHOunQxgyBOvToo~DyfdkRnSa36N4VmztlT6BsJiIHfIeIbM9Wc9vHNevhjPqva8P6IwXzeDnDZBeI87BFwpUi5tMiJ1fwVe3Gl4gFA__&amp;Key-Pair-Id=APKAIE5G5CRDK6RD3PGA\" alt=\"Correlation between total mass density profile slope, \u03b1, and effective radius, Re, (top panel) and average projected total surface mass density within Re/2 (bottom panel). In both panels, the Sa lens J091305.0\u2212005343, coloured with a grey data point, has been omitted in the straight line fit which is shown by the dotted line and grey shaded 1\u03c3 error envelope.\" data-path-from-xml=\"stu305fig4.jpeg\" /><div class=\"fig-orig original-slide\"><a class=\"fig-view-orig js-view-large openInAnotherWindow\" href=\"/view-large/figure/111789771/stu305fig4.jpeg\" data-path-from-xml=\"stu305fig4.jpeg\" target=\"_blank\">Open in new tab</a><a data-section=\"111789771\" href=\"/DownloadFile/DownloadImage.aspx?image=https://oup.silverchair-cdn.com/oup/backfile/Content_public/Journal/mnras/440/3/10.1093_mnras_stu305/3/stu305fig4.jpeg?Expires=1611467636&Signature=HIE64J9QaEBCSkJPZU1B9~Xjgw-tipaGc-EpMAgyV0tdFY3pCMjuD-MiV9bATqHtH20UP21B2HSOgrrlno5kyWwmf50D90TNh6a3FoWY2kPKYJMozFGmLm9M~u8~u4ztmmyJJSuYoC14PooJdGwlVEKnkJCCjGcWvh8i5UHvW61b7BTNqx9QcqXxMqcequOgh2wJ87B5B5~tyMKZXKc2y-PNdcTqj41oAlKGjhwIYWIAg7XmkvZzz1524XGF9q-ZaAHY1Ah9qWSsgr2YuYerP9v8wtTdCLUbfeG7dOFDSUTibHC1UIKEvUllSzgJqWPik~72r1A~qt4bUC71hm5X2Q__&Key-Pair-Id=APKAIE5G5CRDK6RD3PGA&sec=111789771&ar=1074641&xsltPath=~/UI/app/XSLT&imagename=&siteId=5326\" class=\"download-slide\" data-path-from-xml=\"stu305fig4.jpeg\">Download slide</a></div></div><div class=\"caption fig-caption\"><p class=\"chapter-para\">Correlation between total mass density profile slope, \u03b1, and effective radius, <em>R</em><sub>e</sub>, (<em>top panel</em>) and average projected total surface mass density within <em>R</em><sub>e</sub>/2 (<em>bottom panel</em>). In both panels, the Sa lens J091305.0\u2212005343, coloured with a grey data point, has been omitted in the straight line fit which is shown by the dotted line and grey shaded 1\u03c3 error envelope.</p></div></div><p class=\"chapter-para\">In the bottom panel of Fig.\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-fig4\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"fig4\" data-open=\"fig4\" class=\"link link-reveal link-table xref-fig\">4</a>, we plot \u03b1 against the average total surface mass density within half the effective radius, as quantified by the ratio | $M{{\\rm ^{Tot}}_{R_{\\rm e}/2}/({R_{\\rm e}/1\\,{\\rm kpc}})^2}$ |. We obtain a straight line fit of | $\\alpha =11.60\\pm 0.11+ (0.35\\pm 0.22)\\log ({M^{{\\rm Tot}}_{R_{\\rm e}/2}/(R_{\\rm e}/1\\,{\\rm kpc})^2})$ | which compares to the gradient of | ${{\\rm d} \\alpha / {\\rm d} \\log [M^{{\\rm Tot}}_{R_{\\rm e}/2}/(R_{\\rm e}/1\\,{\\rm kpc})^2]=0.85\\pm 0.19}$ | for the SLACS lenses as measured by Auger et\u00a0al. (<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib3\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib3\" data-open=\"bib3\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2010b</a>).</p>                    <h2 scrollto-destination=111789773 id=\"111789773\" class=\"section-title\" data-legacy-id=sec5>5 DISCUSSION</h2>\r\n<p class=\"chapter-para\">As the preceding section has shown, in all observational diagnostics and trends we have considered, bar the correlation between <em>f</em><sub>DM</sub> and | ${R_{\\rm e}}$ |, the H-ATLAS lenses are more similar to the SL2S lenses than those of SLACS. This is perhaps not surprising when one considers the following characteristic median values expressed in order SLACS, SL2S, H-ATLAS: | $\\widetilde{R}_{\\rm e} \\simeq$ |\u00a08, 5, 3\u00a0kpc; | $\\widetilde{M}_* \\simeq 10^{11.6}$ |, 10<sup>11.5</sup>, 10<sup>11.2</sup>\u8201M<sub>\u2299</sub>; | $\\widetilde{M}^{\\rm Tot}_{R_{\\rm e}/2} \\simeq 10^{11.2}$ |, 10<sup>11.0</sup>, 10<sup>10.8</sup>\u8201M<sub>\u2299</sub>. It appears to be the case therefore, at least in the SDP data, that the H-ATLAS lenses populate the low-mass tail of the SL2S lens sample.</p><p class=\"chapter-para\">Despite these obvious differences and despite our very small sample of lenses at present, we still detect many of the correlations found in the various other aforementioned studies. These studies have taken care to ensure that the trends they detect are not the result of selection biases or systematic effects. In a similar vein, a possible systematic effect to be considered when comparing our results with these is that our lensing analysis does not incorporate any additional constraints from dynamical measurements. This means that the slope is measured in the vicinity of the Einstein ring, whereas in analyses using lensing and dynamics, the average slope interior to the Einstein ring is measured. Therefore, a change in slope with radius could potentially introduce a systematic offset in the slopes determined in the present work with respect to those from lensing and dynamics. However, as previously mentioned, on the scales probed by strong lensing, the slope appears not to exhibit any significant dependence on radius since there is no apparent trend in slope with the ratio of Einstein radius to effective radius (Koopmans et\u00a0al. <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib39\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib39\" data-open=\"bib39\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2006</a>; Ruff et\u00a0al. <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib57\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib57\" data-open=\"bib57\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2011</a>).</p><p class=\"chapter-para\">This is related to the effect reported in Ruff et\u00a0al. (<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib57\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib57\" data-open=\"bib57\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2011</a>) and Bolton et\u00a0al. (<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib9\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib9\" data-open=\"bib9\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2012</a>) that the ratio of the Einstein radius to the lens galaxy's effective radius increases with increasing lens redshift. This is due to the redshift dependence of the angular diameter distance ratios which govern the lensing geometry and the fact that a fixed physical size reduces in angular extent with increasing redshift (at least out to the lens redshifts in this work). This has the result that as redshift is increased, the density profile is measured by our lensing-only analysis at a radius which is an increasing multiple of the effective radius. A change in slope with radius would therefore mimic a change in slope with redshift. However, in addition to the observational evidence that the slope is not seen to depend on radius on strong lensing scales, on much larger scales, the slope is expected to steepen with increasing radius according to simulations and the requirement that the total halo mass converges. Therefore, even if this steepening were to influence our slope measurements, our detection of the rate at which slopes become less steep with increasing redshift must be a lower limit to the intrinsic rate.</p><p class=\"chapter-para\">In terms of a physical interpretation of observed variations in the density profile slope, the picture is somewhat unclear. Simulations by Dubois et\u00a0al. (<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib23\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib23\" data-open=\"bib23\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2013</a>) reproduce the observed steepening with decreasing redshift and find that feedback from active galactic nuclei (AGN) modifies the slope. This work indicates that AGN feedback is required to reproduce the near-isothermal profiles (i.e. \u03b1 \u2243 2) observed in low-redshift early-type galaxies. However, the simulations of Remus et\u00a0al. (<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib56\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib56\" data-open=\"bib56\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2013</a>) indicate that whilst a combination of dry minor and major mergers produce near-isothermality at low redshifts, the slopes are significantly steeper at higher redshift. Confounding this is the simulation work of Nipoti, Treu &amp; Bolton (<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib50\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib50\" data-open=\"bib50\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2009</a>) which shows that the total mass profile of early types is not modified at all by dry mergers.</p><p class=\"chapter-para\">Turning to the projected dark matter fraction within half the effective radius, <em>f</em><sub>DM</sub>, Dubois et\u00a0al. (<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib23\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib23\" data-open=\"bib23\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2013</a>) claim that AGN feedback is required to reproduce the observed fractions and that without it, the fraction of stellar mass is too high. The SLACS work reports a 5\u03c3 detection of increasing <em>f</em><sub>DM</sub> with effective radius. This compares to our marginal detection (2.3\u03c3) and a null detection in the SL2S lenses. If this trend is real, an obvious interpretation might be that star formation efficiency reduces as halo mass increases. Another possibility is presented by Nipoti et\u00a0al. (<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib50\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib50\" data-open=\"bib50\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2009</a>) who predict that the fraction of dark matter within the effective radius increases as a result of mergers.</p><p class=\"chapter-para\">Instead of investigating how <em>f</em><sub>DM</sub> varies with effective radius, an alternative is to test whether <em>f</em><sub>DM</sub> changes with redshift since this is another diagnostic which can be provided by simulations.</p><p class=\"chapter-para\">Fig.\u00a0<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-fig5\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"fig5\" data-open=\"fig5\" class=\"link link-reveal link-table xref-fig\">5</a> shows this plot for the H-ATLAS lenses (excluding J091305.0\u2212005343). We measure a straight line fit of <em>f</em><sub>DM</sub>\u00a0=\u00a00.23\u00a0\u00b1 0.09 + (0.32 \u00b1 0.14)| $z$ |. In comparison, the simulations of Dubois et\u00a0al. (<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib23\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib23\" data-open=\"bib23\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2013</a>) predict that the fraction of dark matter within 10\u00a0per\u8201cent of the virial radius decreases with increasing redshift when AGN feedback is present, or, that this fraction remains constant with redshift if AGN feedback is not present. If the fraction of dark matter within 10\u00a0per\u8201cent of the virial radius scales in the same way as <em>f</em><sub>DM</sub>, then this is in contrast to our findings. However, since <em>f</em><sub>DM</sub> depends on the size of the stellar component and the virial radius effectively does not, there is still the possibility that the two results are consistent if the stellar mass increases in spatial extent relative to the dark matter with increasing redshift.</p>                        <a id=\"111789781\" scrollto-destination=\"111789781\"></a>\r\n<div data-id=\"fig5\" class=\"fig fig-section js-fig-section\"><div class=\"label fig-label\">Figure 5.</div><div class=\"graphic-wrap\"><img class=\"content-image\" src=\"https://oup.silverchair-cdn.com/oup/backfile/Content_public/Journal/mnras/440/3/10.1093_mnras_stu305/3/m_stu305fig5.jpeg?Expires=1611467636&amp;Signature=bE44ckpIiptIzXzPbQxMk~jv6tRCl6GNTssjVmJ1ZYCe-3SjXu3Y3SsNN8JsQ4iy9h9SoU1UwU-FroBqDwMYi8EzvoXkDvWC3-YtuSrW7qIhg6tHKnSA4EoMJ2~yw3PQOopQeFslQZU-ltR3Q6297aa1QkYmNXBM-e5ryIieTkbavSIS2hYwdPESqJejlZj9fnYz80vSRjv4cCM8qEWc8QaIhy1nQhFQwiabewrC~2cNMz5KC17frChdNFBaB6Oa-HjCHhETNuI1fXFml4KeEjH13idy6TpwpyPcAh6EDIuu5Nlhmmah4rJkQvhR~DHhztuWj~eytsrhYWdj07v-kg__&amp;Key-Pair-Id=APKAIE5G5CRDK6RD3PGA\" alt=\"Variation of the fraction of dark matter within half the effective radius, fDM, for a Salpeter IMF with redshift. The grey shading depicts the 1\u03c3 error region for the straight line fit.</p></div></div><div content-id=\"fig5\" class=\"fig fig-modal reveal-modal\"><div class=\"label fig-label\">Figure 5.</div><div class=\"graphic-wrap\"><img class=\"content-image\" src=\"https://oup.silverchair-cdn.com/oup/backfile/Content_public/Journal/mnras/440/3/10.1093_mnras_stu305/3/m_stu305fig5.jpeg?Expires=1611467636&amp;Signature=bE44ckpIiptIzXzPbQxMk~jv6tRCl6GNTssjVmJ1ZYCe-3SjXu3Y3SsNN8JsQ4iy9h9SoU1UwU-FroBqDwMYi8EzvoXkDvWC3-YtuSrW7qIhg6tHKnSA4EoMJ2~yw3PQOopQeFslQZU-ltR3Q6297aa1QkYmNXBM-e5ryIieTkbavSIS2hYwdPESqJejlZj9fnYz80vSRjv4cCM8qEWc8QaIhy1nQhFQwiabewrC~2cNMz5KC17frChdNFBaB6Oa-HjCHhETNuI1fXFml4KeEjH13idy6TpwpyPcAh6EDIuu5Nlhmmah4rJkQvhR~DHhztuWj~eytsrhYWdj07v-kg__&amp;Key-Pair-Id=APKAIE5G5CRDK6RD3PGA\" alt=\"Variation of the fraction of dark matter within half the effective radius, fDM, for a Salpeter IMF with redshift. The grey shading depicts the 1\u03c3 error region for the straight line fit.\" data-path-from-xml=\"stu305fig5.jpeg\" /><div class=\"fig-orig original-slide\"><a class=\"fig-view-orig js-view-large openInAnotherWindow\" href=\"/view-large/figure/111789781/stu305fig5.jpeg\" data-path-from-xml=\"stu305fig5.jpeg\" target=\"_blank\">Open in new tab</a><a data-section=\"111789781\" href=\"/DownloadFile/DownloadImage.aspx?image=https://oup.silverchair-cdn.com/oup/backfile/Content_public/Journal/mnras/440/3/10.1093_mnras_stu305/3/stu305fig5.jpeg?Expires=1611467636&Signature=vXfodpP8pG0RDn2oKI9TNKsLnL4qCqNnqwrWwgok9sH2Q9lSrR9p4dRabH1KG~6D0OgSaFMA8Prm3lb~W3xp13jN9Ga4EoGkDgLmJgae5PHcatAC5Y~PtZzeeCBC3BZPJ6Nqld7kZlN2YYf~zwJLhhEfk2TlWKMf4SwMWgzz3UYLqtVJxaxss88yG2svvqtkujo~LtOHnrj8E8~Ls-3trapqQTmdWa8cUECVlU~EYyv~uyOb5KAC0hB13ix2xXmzHi0XW9zQ0odYVnSMYHWUUg-surv23LkCq7tIEftyjj8z2hxsDti-Q0GOBPNY6l1ETKHM8fN~ORVpdfyodBPuKQ__&Key-Pair-Id=APKAIE5G5CRDK6RD3PGA&sec=111789781&ar=1074641&xsltPath=~/UI/app/XSLT&imagename=&siteId=5326\" class=\"download-slide\" data-path-from-xml=\"stu305fig5.jpeg\">Download slide</a></div></div><div class=\"caption fig-caption\"><p class=\"chapter-para\">Variation of the fraction of dark matter within half the effective radius, <em>f</em><sub>DM</sub>, for a Salpeter IMF with redshift. The grey shading depicts the 1\u03c3 error region for the straight line fit.</p></div></div><p class=\"chapter-para\">Important clues also come from comparing the morphology of the visible component of the lenses with that of the dark matter halo. We find significant discrepancies in the alignment and ellipticity between the stellar component and the total mass in some lenses. The discrepancies are consistent with what has been measured in the SL2S sample but larger than those found in SLACS. This may be a combination of the fact that both the H-ATLAS and SL2S lenses have a higher average ratio of Einstein radius to effective radius than the SLACS lenses and that the baryonic morphology correlates less strongly with that of the dark matter at larger radii (e.g. Bett et\u00a0al. <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib6\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib6\" data-open=\"bib6\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2010</a>; Skibba &amp; Macci\u00f2 <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib61\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib61\" data-open=\"bib61\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2011</a>). In order to proceed with a more robust interpretation of these findings, more input is required from simulation work although as we previously discussed, present indications are that such large morphological differences between the dark and baryonic components are to be expected.</p>                    <h2 scrollto-destination=111789783 id=\"111789783\" class=\"section-title\" data-legacy-id=sec6>6 SUMMARY</h2>\r\n<p class=\"chapter-para\">In this paper, we have modelled the first five strong gravitational lens systems discovered in the H-ATLAS SDP data. To directly compare with other lensing studies, we have modelled the lenses with elliptical power-law density profiles and searched for trends in the power-law slope and the fraction of dark matter contained within half the effective radius. We have found consistency with almost all existing lens analyses, although with our present sample of only five lenses, we lack high statistical significance in our measured trends. The main results of this paper are that: <ul class=\"roman-lower\"><li><p class=\"chapter-para\">the slope of the power-law density profile varies with redshift according to \u03b1\u00a0=\u00a02.05 \u00b1 0.08\u00a0\u2212\u00a0(0.30 \u00b1 0.14)| $z$ |.</p></li><li><p class=\"chapter-para\">the H-ATLAS lenses have a mean projected dark matter fraction within half the effective radius of <em>f</em><sub>DM</sub>\u00a0=\u00a00.46 with a scatter of 0.10, for a Salpeter IMF;</p></li><li><p class=\"chapter-para\">the dark matter fraction within half the effective radius scales with effective radius as | $f_{\\rm DM}=-0.01\\pm 0.20 + (1.04\\pm 0.46)\\log ({R_{\\rm e}/1\\,{\\rm kpc}})$ |;</p></li><li><p class=\"chapter-para\">the slope of the power-law density profile scales with effective radius as | $\\alpha =2.17\\pm 0.20 - (0.70\\pm 0.47)\\log ({R_{\\rm e} / 1\\,{\\rm kpc}})$ | and with the average total surface mass density within half the effective radius, as quantified by the ratio | $M{^{{\\rm Tot}}_{R_{\\rm e}/2}/(R_{\\rm e}/1\\,{\\rm kpc})^2}$ | as | $M{^{{\\rm Tot}}_{R_{\\rm e}/2}/(R_{\\rm e}/1\\,{\\rm kpc})^2}$ |;</p></li><li><p class=\"chapter-para\"><em>f</em><sub>DM</sub> scales with redshift as <em>f</em><sub>DM</sub>\u00a0=\u00a00.23 \u00b1 0.09 + (0.32 \u00b1 0.14)| $z$ |.</p></li></ul></p><p class=\"chapter-para\">The modelling in this paper used near-IR <em>HST</em> data. Whilst the <em>HST</em> provides the high-resolution imaging necessary for modelling of high-redshift lenses, not all of the H-ATLAS lensed sources will be as readily detected in the near-IR as the SDP lenses considered herein. Being submm selected systems, submm and radio interferometry is the ideal technology for obtaining the required signal to noise and image resolution. This has been demonstrated by (<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib14\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib14\" data-open=\"bib14\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">B13</a>) who have used the SMA to image several tens of lenses detected by the <em>Herschel Space Observatory</em>. Atacama Large Millimetre Array (ALMA) has also been used to image some of the SPT lenses (see, for example Hezaveh et\u00a0al. <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib33\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib33\" data-open=\"bib33\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">2013</a>). However, the true power of this facility will not be realized until it operates with its full complement of antennas. At this point, ALMA will begin to deliver the high signal-to-noise and high-resolution images required by source-inversion lens modelling methods, necessary for the strongest possible constraints on galaxy mass profiles. Furthermore, spectral line imaging with ALMA will open up the possibility of reconstructing lensed source velocity maps to probe the dynamics of high-redshift submm galaxies.</p><p class=\"chapter-para\">The H-ATLAS lens sample is very much in its infancy. As the size of the sample grows and begins to populate the | $z$ |<sub>d</sub> \u2243 1 realm and beyond, constraints on the evolution of mass in galaxies will continue to strengthen.</p><p class=\"chapter-para\">The work in this paper is based on observations made with the NASA/ESA <em>Hubble Space Telescope</em> under the <em>HST</em> programme #12194. MN acknowledges financial support from ASI/INAF Agreement I/072/09/0 and from PRIN-INAF 2012 project: \u2018Looking into the dust-obscured phase of galaxy formation through cosmic zoom lenses in the Herschel Astrophysical Large Area Survey\u2019. JGN acknowledges financial support from the Spanish Ministerio de Ciencia e Innovacion, project AYA2010-21766-C03-01, and the Spanish CSIC for a JAE-DOC fellowship, co-funded by the European Social Fund. We thank Martin Baes and Michal Michalowski for constructive comments on this paper. We would also like to thank Adam Moss for technical discussions regarding the MCMC analysis contained herein.</p><div content-id=\"fn1\"><span class=\"fn\"><span class=\"label fn-label\"><span rel=\"nofollow\" data-fn-id=\"fn1\" class=\"end-note-link\">\u2605</span></span><div class=\"footnote-content\"><p class=\"footnote-compatibility\"><em>Herschel</em> is an ESA space observatory with science instruments provided by European-led Principal Investigator consortia and with important participation from NASA.</p></div></span></div><div content-id=\"fn2\"><span class=\"fn\"><span class=\"label fn-label\"><span rel=\"nofollow\" data-fn-id=\"fn2\" class=\"end-note-link\">1</span></span><div class=\"footnote-content\"><p class=\"footnote-compatibility\">In practice, we have opted to set the same regularization weight across all images to simplify the MCMC minimization.</p></div></span></div><div content-id=\"fn3\"><span class=\"fn\"><span class=\"label fn-label\"><span rel=\"nofollow\" data-fn-id=\"fn3\" class=\"end-note-link\">2</span></span><div class=\"footnote-content\"><p class=\"footnote-compatibility\">We note that the lens elongations derived by <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib14\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib14\" data-open=\"bib14\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">B13</a> are consistently higher than those derived in our study and we attribute this, at least partly, to the lack of external shear in the <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib14\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib14\" data-open=\"bib14\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">B13</a> lens model.</p></div></span></div><div content-id=\"fn4\"><span class=\"fn\"><span class=\"label four-dimensional plane that Sonnenfeld et\u00a0al. fit to the density profile slope by assuming a redshift of 0.3 and a stellar mass of | $\\log ({M_*/{\\rm M}_{\\odot }})=11.5$ |.</p></div></span></div>                    <h2 scrollto-destination=111789794 id=\"111789794\" class=\"backreferences-title\" >REFERENCES</h2>\r\n<div class=\"ref-list\"><div content-id=\"bib1a\" J.</div>\r\n</span>\r\n<span class=\"name string-name\">\r\n<div class=\"surname\">Dye</div>\r\n <div class=\"given-names\">S</div>\r\n</span>\r\n</span>.\r\n<div class=\"source \">ApJ</div>., <div class=\"year\">2003</div>, <div class=\"volume\">590</div>, <div class=\"fpage\">673</div>.\r\n</p><!--citationLinks: case 1--><div class=\"citation-links\"></div><div class=\"citation-links\"><div class=\"crossref-doi js-ref-link\"><a class=\"openInAnotherWindow\" href=\"http://dx.doi.org/10.1086/apj.2003.590.issue-2\" target=\"_blank\">Crossref</a></div><div class=\"adsDoiReference\"><a class=\"openInAnotherWindow\" href=\"http://adsabs.harvard.edu/cgi-bin/basic_connect?qsearch=10.1086%2Fapj.2003.590.issue-2\" target=\"_blank\">Search ADS</a></div><div class=\"xslopenurl empty-target\"><span class=\"inst-open-url-holders\" data-targetId=\"10.1086%2Fapj.2003.590.issue-2\"> </span></div>\u00a0</div></div></div></div></div></div>                    <h3 scrollto-destination=111789796 id=\"111789796\" class=\"appendix-title\" data-legacy-id=sec7>APPENDIX A: LENS PARAMETER CONFIDENCE PLOTS</h3>\r\n<p class=\"chapter-para\">In this appendix, we plot the confidence contours for all parameter combinations for each lens (apart from the lens position parameters <em>x</em><sub>c</sub> and <em>y</em><sub>c</sub> since we did not detect any significant offsets between the lens mass centre and the centroid of the lens galaxy light). In each plot, the contours correspond to the 1, 2 and 3\u03c3 confidence levels.</p>                        <a id=\"111789798\" scrollto-destination=\"111789798\"></a>\r\n<div data-id=\"fig6\" class=\"fig fig-section js-fig-section\"><div class=\"label fig-label\">Figure A1.</div><div class=\"graphic-wrap\"><img class=\"content-image\" src=\"https://oup.silverchair-cdn.com/oup/backfile/Content_public/Journal/mnras/440/3/10.1093_mnras_stu305/3/m_stu305figa1.jpeg?Expires=1611467636&amp;Signature=TAEYP6CtzMMUe-LqLNk2syylb2bCNNf02ZvFYh~Cably5Jaapi31i39i-QxH7rzOwzEufRuXemPMcMCPc7exmXWViA6KYSm87XJx9s~skg4g7oZLS1p9oNbTsOY0mI6Ltc~-BsYgC33GO25WtcO~1teg2W7JPLewYYoLMuR6xWV5acDqGZotn5ci4Vm5awMBG3IbMmek7qjUNY9sX3StiS2HRfz1y3XFTwXG8wKJ1m85rrOZM-lGO9BQlbYW-2iHzix08cAPdDhzuEe1t6PZyOvq-pgplZFNNEN9JPBQ77pVwD35zGCH6cPOY4FAjAiTer6pJzbWC-Sr8mAS-qIf0A__&amp;Key-Pair-Id=APKAIE5G5CRDK6RD3PGA\" alt=\"Parameter confidence limits. "https://ui.adsabs.harvard.edu/abs/2014MNRAS.440.2013D", "Tables status": "Complete", "Inspection status": "Incomplete", "Paper Overview": {"abstract": "We have determined the mass density radial profiles of the first five strong gravitational lens systems discovered by the Herschel Astrophysical Terahertz Large Area Survey. We present an enhancement of the semilinear lens inversion method of Warren &amp; Dye which allows simultaneous reconstruction of several different wavebands and apply this to dual-band imaging of the lenses acquired with the Hubble Space Telescope. The five systems analysed here have lens redshifts which span a range 0.22 \u2264 z \u2264 0.94. Our findings are consistent with other studies by concluding that: (1) the logarithmic slope of the total mass density profile steepens with decreasing redshift; (2) the slope is positively correlated with the average total projected mass density of the lens contained within half the effective radius and negatively correlated with the effective radius; (3) the fraction of dark matter contained within half the effective radius increases with increasing effective radius and increases with redshift.", "start_page": "2013", "authors": ["Dye, S.", "Negrello, M.", "Hopwood, R.", "Nightingale, J.
